# Chapter 1: Introduction

## 1.1 Purpose and Need for the Plan  
*WAC 173-240-050 (a)*

The Town of Spangle seeks to develop a General Sewer Plan and Facility Plan to address current and future wastewater management needs in compliance with Washington Administrative Code (WAC) 173-240-050 and WAC 173-240-060. This plan will ensure that the Town’s wastewater collection and treatment systems continue to operate efficiently, meet regulatory requirements, and support planned growth over the next 20 years.

The development of this plan is driven by the need to:  
* Evaluate existing system performance, capacity, and infrastructure conditions.  
* Identify necessary improvements to address aging infrastructure, inflow and infiltration (I&I) concerns, and system deficiencies.  
* Assess future population growth and land use changes to ensure the system can accommodate projected demand.  
* Ensure compliance with Washington State Department of Ecology’s (Ecology) General Engineering Requirements and discharge standards.  
* Develop cost-effective and sustainable solutions for system expansion and upgrades.

## 1.2 System Ownership, Operation, and Maintenance  
*WAC 173-240-050 (b)*

---

The Town of Spangle owns and operates the wastewater treatment plant (WWTP) and sewer collection system, which includes gravity sewers, force mains, and lift stations. The Town’s Public Works Department is responsible for the system’s operation and maintenance, ensuring compliance with state and federal environmental regulations. As the Town continues to grow, strategic planning is essential to maintain the reliability and efficiency of wastewater services while minimizing environmental impacts.

* Address: P.O. Box 147, Spangle, WA 99031  
* Mayor: Melissa Holling  
* WWTP Location: 675 N. Old SR 195, Spangle, WA  
* System Operator: Logan Billington  

# 1.3 Key Issues and Intended Outcomes  
WAC 173-240-050 (a)

Several key issues necessitate the development of this Facility Plan, including:

* Capacity and Condition Assessment: Evaluating the condition of the existing collection and treatment infrastructure and identifying deficiencies that may limit system performance.  
* Regulatory Compliance: Ensuring adherence to Ecology’s discharge standards and permitting requirements.  
* Future Growth Considerations: Incorporating population projections, industrial and commercial development, and urban growth area (UGA) expansion into system planning.  
* Hydraulic and Treatment Capacity: Assessing the hydraulic capacity of the collection system and treatment plant to support future demand.  
* Financial Feasibility: Developing a capital improvement plan (CIP) with cost estimates, potential funding strategies, and rate impacts.  
* The Facility Plan will provide a comprehensive roadmap for system improvements, ensuring the Town’s wastewater infrastructure remains functional, cost-effective, and environmentally responsible for the next two decades.  

# 1.4 Background  
## 1.4.1 Location and Geography  
WAC 173-240-050 (d)

Spangle is a small, rural town located in the eastern part of Washington State, approximately 20 miles south of Spokane and 57 miles north of Pullman. Situated in Spokane County,

The town covers an area of 0.36 square miles and is home to a local residents, as well as some agricultural and light commercial development. Spangle is positioned within the Spokane Metropolitan Area, which contributes to its long-term growth prospects. The surrounding region has experienced steady population increases in recent years, leading to a higher demand for infrastructure improvements and municipal services.

## 1.4.2 Vicinity Map  
WAC 173-240-050 (d)(i)

---

Figure 1.1 provides a vicinity map of Spangle, showing its location in relation to major highways and the city of Spokane. Spangle is situated approximately 18 miles south of downtown Spokane along U.S. Route 195, a key north-south highway. The map highlights Spangle's position within the larger Spokane metropolitan area.

### 1.4.3 Topography and Hydrology  
WAC 173-240-050 (v)

The topography of Spangle is defined by a relatively gentle slope, with the town being bisected by Spangle Creek, an intermittent creek that flows from the southeast to the northwest. The elevation at the southeastern corner of the city limits is 2,490 feet, marking the highest point within the area. Moving toward the northwest, the elevation decreases to 2,410 feet, creating a total elevation difference of approximately 80 feet across the town (Figure 1.2).

The Spangle Creek runs diagonally through the town, dividing it into two distinct sections. The terrain on both sides of the creek gently slopes toward the creek bed in the middle, contributing to the overall topographical gradient. The slope is gradual, and the elevation decreases uniformly as it approaches the creek. This topographical feature significantly influences the sewer collection patterns and potential sewer collection and management considerations within the town.

Overall, the town’s topography is characterized by moderate elevation changes, with the creek serving as a central feature dividing the landscape. The gentle relief of the area will guide the planning and design of infrastructure systems, particularly with regard to the management of wastewater collection and transport and the alignment of utility systems.

Spangle is situated within the Hangman Creek watershed hydrological region, which is important for stormwater management, groundwater recharge, and wastewater treatment and discharge. The town’s proximity to Spangle Creek has been considered in the planning of the wastewater system, especially regarding treatment standards and discharge permits (Figure 1.3).

The existing wastewater treatment plant in the City of Spangle is not located within a designated flood hazard area, as shown in the floodplain map included in the Appendix D. While the identified flood hazard area follows the alignment of Spangle Creek through parts of the city, the treatment plant is situated outside this zone, minimizing the risk of flood-related impacts and supporting reliable facility operation during high-water events.

# 1.5 Overview of the Facility Plan

This Facility Plan is structured to provide a comprehensive understanding of Spangle’s wastewater management needs, as well as the key criteria influencing the design and development of the system. The chapters of the Facility Plan are organized to address various aspects of the current system and guide decision-making for future improvements.

Chapters 2 – focuses on analyzing and projecting population growth for Spangle. It discusses the town’s historical population trends and explores two projection methods: one based on organic socio-economic development and the other focusing on the impact of the Wheatland Estates housing project. The chapter examines factors such as housing data, growth rates, and potential future developments to estimate how Spangle’s population might change over time.

Chapters 3 – This chapter provides an overview of the regulatory framework governing wastewater planning, with a focus on federal, state, and local compliance requirements. It highlights the Clean Water Act (CWA) and the National Pollutant Discharge Elimination System (NPDES) permit, which regulate wastewater discharges in Washington State under the Department of Ecology’s administration. The chapter details specific water quality standards for surface and groundwater, effluent discharge limits, and compliance measures for municipal wastewater treatment plants, including Spangle’s sewage treatment facility. Additionally, it examines Spangle Creek’s designated uses, current water quality issues, and the need for a receiving water study and potential Total Maximum Daily Load (TMDL) allocations to address ongoing environmental concerns.

Chapter 4 – This chapter provides an overview of the existing wastewater collection, treatment, and disposal system in Spangle. It details the components of the system, including gravity sewers, a lift station, force mains, and the wastewater treatment facility. The chapter also discusses the history, treatment processes, regulatory oversight, and effluent discharge into Spangle Creek. Additionally, it covers system design criteria and effluent limitations to ensure compliance with regulatory standards.

Chapter 5 – This chapter provides an estimate of wastewater flow and strength loads for the town of Spangle. It discusses key flow parameters, including Base Sanitary Flow (BSF), Average Dry Weather Flow (ADWF), Maximum Dry Weather Flow (MDWF), Average Wet Weather Flow (AWWF), Maximum Week Flow, and Peak Hourly and Instantaneous Design Flow. These parameters are used to estimate wastewater flow under different conditions, helping to assess the current sewer system's capacity and plan for future demand. The chapter also examines wastewater strength, focusing on parameters such as Biochemical Oxygen Demand (BOD₅) and Total Suspended Solids (TSS). These strength indicators are essential for evaluating the pollutant load and ensuring that treatment systems are designed to handle varying wastewater strength.

---

Chapter 6, Collection System Improvements, evaluates Spangle's existing sewer infrastructure and its ability to accommodate future growth. The current collection system, including the lift station, is assessed for its capacity to handle both existing and projected wastewater flows. The chapter examines two growth scenarios: organic development and expansion driven by the Wheatland Estates project, using a hydraulic model to determine if the current system will be sufficient.

---

# Chapter 2: Wastewater Planning Area Characteristic

## 2.1 Land Use and Zoning  
WAC 173-240-050 (d)(i)

Spangle's zoning map, as shown in Figure 2-1 designates three land use categories: residential, commercial, and agricultural. As Spangle grows, zoning may change, affecting both population density and development patterns, which will influence wastewater demand. The wastewater facility plan must take into account both existing zoning and any anticipated changes to ensure the system can handle future growth. Regular updates will help ensure the wastewater infrastructure continues to meet the needs of the community while supporting planned land uses.

## 2.2 Urban Growth Areas (UGAs) and Future Development in Spangle  
WAC 173-240-050 (d)(i)

An Urban Growth Area (UGA) is a region designated for future urban development by local planning authorities. The purpose of UGAs is to manage the expansion of cities or towns in a way that is both planned and controlled, while also preserving surrounding rural and agricultural lands. These areas are intended for future residential, commercial, or industrial development to accommodate the needs of a growing population. UGAs will include sufficient land and densities to permit the urban growth that is projected to occur in the next 20 years.

In Spokane County, UGAs play a critical role in the overall land-use strategy, as established under the Growth Management Act (GMA). The GMA emphasizes that urban development should be concentrated in areas with sufficient infrastructure and services, thus minimizing the spread of urban sprawl into rural regions. Each city, town, and unincorporated area within the county is assigned a UGA, which is crucial to ensuring that urban development is sustainable, compatible with existing resources, and addresses environmental concerns and community character.

In Spangle, two designated UGAs, shown in Figure 2.2, are considered for future urban growth. One UGA is located on the south side of the existing city limits, and the other lies just outside the current city boundaries. However, as of now, no housing or commercial development is planned for these UGA areas. It is important to distinguish that the Wheatland Estates housing development, a significant growth project, is located within the city limits and not within the designated UGAs. This distinction emphasizes the current development focus within the existing urban area rather than extending into the surrounding UGAs.

## 2.3 Geography and Landscape of Spangle, Washington  
WAC 173-240-050 (f)

Spangle, Washington, is located in southeastern Spokane County, approximately 20 miles south of the city of Spokane. It lies within the Palouse region, a unique and picturesque area characterized by rolling hills and fertile farmland. The town is situated at an elevation of approximately 2,400

---

feet (700 meters) above sea level, providing it with a somewhat elevated view of the surrounding countryside. Spangle is bordered by several small creeks and rivers, contributing to the area's natural beauty and agricultural productivity. The town's geographic coordinates are 47°25'49.27"N latitude and 117°22'46"W longitude.

The landscape around Spangle is dominated by expansive wheat fields, as the Palouse is known for its wheat-growing potential due to the region's rich, loess soil. The area's topography consists of gently sloping hills, valleys, and draws, which are a result of ancient glacial activity. These geographical features create a scenic and agricultural environment while also influencing water drainage patterns and the local climate.

The town is positioned within a semi-arid climate zone, experiencing hot, dry summers and cold, snowy winters. This climate, combined with its fertile soil, makes the surrounding area ideal for agriculture, particularly wheat farming. The proximity of Spangle to Spokane also means it is influenced by the growth of the larger metropolitan area, which may affect its future development patterns.

To identify any existing domestic or industrial wastewater facilities within a 20-mile radius of the Spangle wastewater treatment and sewer infrastructure area—and within the same topographical drainage basin as the general planning area—the USGS StreamStats software was used to delineate the watershed contributing to Spangle Creek. After delineation, the watershed boundary was exported as a shapefile and imported into a ArcGIS platform for further analysis. Based on this investigation, the only wastewater facility located within the same topographical drainage basin is Upper Columbia Academy wastewater treatment facility.

## 2.4 Weather in Spangle, Washington

In Spangle, the climate is semi-arid, characterized by hot, dry summers and cold, snowy winters. The temperature typically varies from 26°F in winter to 88°F in summer, with extreme temperatures rarely falling below 11°F or exceeding 97°F.

The hot season lasts for 2.8 months, from June 19 to September 12, with average daily highs above 78°F. The hottest month is July, with an average high of 86°F and a low of 58°F.

The cold season lasts for 3.3 months, from November 14 to February 23, with average daily highs below 45°F. The coldest month is December, with an average low of 27°F and a high of 36°F.

Precipitation in Spangle is more common during the wetter season, which lasts 7.6 months from October 21 to June 7. The drier season lasts 4.4 months, from June 7 to October 21. The month with the most rain is November, receiving an average of 2.4 inches. August is the driest month, with just 0.5 inches of rain on average.

Snowfall occurs mainly during the winter months, from November 13 to March 9. December is the snowiest month, with an average of 4.3 inches of snow. The snowless period spans from March 9 to November 13.

---

The variation in precipitation and snowfall is influenced by Spangle's semi-arid climate and location in the Palouse region, which is known for its agricultural potential due to its fertile soils and favorable climate for wheat farming.

The weather data is sourced from the National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration (NOAA), which conducted a statistical analysis of historical hourly weather reports and model reconstructions from January 1, 1980, to December 31, 2016.

## 2.5 Endangered Species and Priority Habitat Assessment

As part of the environmental review for this Sewer Facility Plan, a Priority Habitats and Species (PHS) Report was obtained from the Washington Department of Fish and Wildlife (WDFW), dated April 18, 2025. The report identifies mapped occurrences of Shrubsteppe and Eastside Steppe habitat types within the project vicinity. These areas are classified as PHS-listed habitat features but are not designated as sensitive and are not associated with any federally or state-listed endangered species.

Additionally, a review of the U.S. Fish and Wildlife Service’s official species list, also dated April 18, 2025, identified five federally listed species that may occur within or near the project area: the Yellow-billed Cuckoo (Threatened), Bull Trout (Threatened), Monarch Butterfly (Proposed Threatened), Suckley’s Cuckoo Bumble Bee (Proposed Endangered), and Spalding’s Catchfly (Threatened). However, according to the species list, the project area does not overlap with any designated critical habitat for these species.

## 2.6 Wetlands

The U.S. Fish and Wildlife Service Wetland Inventory Map has been utilized to identify wetlands in the proximity of the proposed treatment plant. As shown in the map provided in the Appendix G, the treatment plant lagoon has been classified as Wetland PUBHX. Additionally, two riverine wetlands, classified as R4SBC, were identified to the south and north of the treatment plant site. The R4SBC classification refers to a seasonally flooded streambed in an intermittent riverine system, where water flows only part of the year, typically flooding during early growing seasons but receding by the end of the season.

## 2.7 Water System Description

WAC 173-240-050 (vii)

The Town of Spangle owns and operates a Group A public water system (DOH ID: 828707) that serves the residents within the municipal boundaries. The system includes two active groundwater wells, two storage reservoirs, and a network of distribution and transmission mains. The system operates within a single pressure zone.

### 2.7.1 Water Sources

The Town's water supply is derived from two chlorinated groundwater wells, each equipped with submersible pumps and enclosed in well houses:

---

* Well SO3, constructed in 1977, is located at 410 W 1st Street, near the Harvester Restaurant. It has a pumping capacity of approximately 105 gallons per minute (gpm).
* Well SO4, constructed in 1989, is located at the corner of E 4th Street and N Spangle Avenue, with a higher pumping capacity of approximately 269 gpm.

Both wells draw from the Spokane Valley-Rathdrum Prairie aquifer system, providing high-quality groundwater with minimal treatment beyond chlorination for disinfection.

## 2.7.2 Water Storage
The water system includes two elevated steel reservoirs situated about ¼ mile east of the Town on a hilltop to provide gravity-fed pressure to the system:

* The 75,000-gallon reservoir, constructed in 1954, serves as the original storage structure. It is still in service and maintained for redundancy.
* The 100,000-gallon reservoir, constructed in 1977, is the primary storage facility for the system.

Together, these tanks provide 175,000 gallons of total storage, supporting both operational supply and fire flow demands. Water is conveyed from the tanks to the distribution system via an 8-inch transmission main, which connects to the town grid near E 3rd Street.

## 2.8 Distribution System
The distribution system consists of ductile iron and PVC pipes, ranging in size from 4 inches to 8 inches, with the majority being 4- and 6-inch mains. The system includes fire hydrants, valves, and service connections. As of the latest count, the system has 143 metered services, including residential, commercial, and irrigation users, with 13 unmetered connections still present.

# 2.9 Soil Characteristics  
WAC 173-240-060(4)(a)

The soil information for the Spangle Facility Plan was obtained from the USDA Web Soil Survey, which provides detailed soil data for land-use and infrastructure planning. This data covers both developed and undeveloped areas within the city limits of Spangle (Figure 2.3: Soil Map). The soil types identified in the survey help inform decisions regarding land suitability for various uses, such as infrastructure development. The survey provides valuable insights into soil properties such as drainage, texture, and limitations, allowing for better planning and management of the land within Spangle. The suitability of soil for agricultural wastewater disposal through irrigation, overland flow and sludge disposal was evaluated based on various factors such as water movement, depth to saturated zones, and slope characteristics. As shown in Figure 2-3: Soil Map, the soil surrounding the Wastewater Treatment Plant—Naff Silt Loam—is considered the most suitable soil for the agricultural use of treated effluent. While this soil is ideal for irrigation, its effectiveness is somewhat limited. The soil is rated as "somewhat limited" for irrigation due to its slow water movement (0.22 in/hr), which reduces its capacity to efficiently absorb and distribute wastewater. While it can support some level of irrigation, rapid infiltration remains restricted.

For overland flow, Naff Silt Loam is rated as "very limited", primarily due to issues such as seepage (1.00) and slow water movement. These factors hinder the soil's ability to manage wastewater effectively through overland flow, leading to inadequate retention and drainage. Therefore, using this soil for overland flow is not recommended, as it may lead to environmental concerns.

Naff Silt Loam is classified as prime farmland and is well-drained, with a moderately high capacity for water transmission in its most limiting layer. However, the slow water movement and seepage issues require careful consideration of hydrological and drainage management practices to minimize environmental impacts and ensure effective wastewater disposal.

## 2.10 Geohydrologic Condition  
*WAC 173-240-060(4)(b)(i)*

The Town of Spangle is located within the Columbia Plateau regional aquifer system, a major groundwater system that underlies parts of Washington, Oregon, and Idaho. This aquifer system is primarily composed of Miocene-aged basalt flows from the Columbia River Basalt Group, interbedded with sedimentary deposits, and overlain in many areas by unconsolidated sediments referred to as the "overburden aquifer."

### 2.10.1 Columbia Plateau Aquifer System Overview  
The Columbia Plateau aquifer system comprises three major basalt formations that serve as aquifers:

* Saddle Mountains Basalt (youngest)  
* Wanapum Basalt  
* Grande Ronde Basalt (most voluminous)  

---

These formations are separated by confining layers of fine-grained sediments and interbeds. Above the basalts lies the overburden aquifer, consisting of fluvial, lacustrine, volcanic, and eolian sediments, which is typically more permeable than the basalt units.

## 2.10.2 Groundwater Flow and Recharge
Groundwater generally flows from upland recharge zones toward surface water features and discharge zones. The regional flow direction near Spangle is likely southwest, in line with the regional dip of basalt and land slope. Recharge occurs from precipitation, irrigation return flows, and infiltration from streams.

## 2.10.3 Groundwater Use and Quality
Water in the overburden aquifer is commonly used for single resident domestic wells. Deeper wells may tap into Wanapum or Grande Ronde basalt units. Groundwater quality in basalt units is typically good, dominated by calcium-magnesium-bicarbonate types. However, areas with thin overburden and surface irrigation are at risk of nitrate contamination, which is a consideration for effluent disposal.

## 2.10.4 Relevance to Sewer Facility Planning
When planning sewer facilities, it is important to consider that the overburden aquifer—because of its higher permeability—is more vulnerable to contamination from potential leaks in sewer lines or unlined treatment lagoons. Site selection for any infiltration basins or lagoons should take into account the depth to groundwater and the presence of confining soil or rock layers that can limit vertical flow. In order to protect domestic wells and the deeper basalt aquifers, site-specific hydrogeologic studies are recommended to assess the risk of groundwater contamination. In addition, managing nitrogen levels in the treated effluent will be essential.

## 2.10.5 Site-Specific Subsurface Conditions
Subsurface investigations from several well logs and soil borings within the Town of Spangle and nearby areas, as shown in Appendix H, show a consistent geologic pattern that matches the regional Columbia Plateau aquifer system. The average upper 2 to 60 feet consist primarily of unconsolidated overburden materials, such as sandy or silty clay, loess, and topsoil—typically brown to tan in color and varying from soft to hard. These sediments represent the overburden aquifer and serve as the initial zone of groundwater interaction. Beneath this layer, basalt becomes the dominant lithology, often fractured or interbedded with clay seams, which serve as secondary aquifers or confining units depending on their permeability and connectivity.

Basalt units identified in the logs range from soft to hard and include brown, black, and gray varieties, suggesting different flow units of the Columbia River Basalt Group. Notably, water-bearing zones were encountered within fractured basalt at depths ranging from 54 to 151 feet, confirming that the underlying Wanapum and Grande Ronde basalts act as confined aquifers in this region. These stratified basalt and shale formations provide some natural protection to

---

deeper aquifers but may also channel horizontal flow if effluent enters through permeable zones above.

The variability of the overburden thickness and the presence of water-bearing fractures in relatively shallow basalt layers heighten the importance of careful effluent disposal planning. Infiltration systems located in areas with thin overburden or fractured basalt near the surface pose a risk of direct transmission of contaminants into groundwater. The potential for nitrate migration, particularly in areas subject to surface irrigation or with sandy upper soils, is a critical consideration for long-term aquifer protection. Site-specific geotechnical and hydrogeologic evaluations are therefore essential for any proposed infiltration or lagoon-based treatment systems to assess the permeability, depth to water table, and natural confining conditions.

A geotechnical soil boring conducted at the proposed land application site in the City of Spangle revealed approximately 17 feet of loess underlain by a hard basalt layer. The loess, a wind-deposited silty soil, is moderately permeable and can support vertical infiltration of effluent. However, the presence of unweathered basalt directly below this layer creates a sharp boundary that may impede downward percolation and promote lateral movement of water along the top of the basalt.

To reduce the risk of lateral effluent migration, several design and operational controls should be implemented. First, effluent application rates must be carefully managed to remain below the soil’s infiltration capacity and avoid saturation of the loess. Resting periods between application events should be incorporated to allow the soil to dry and re-oxygenate, maintaining its ability to absorb and treat the effluent. In addition, grading the site to promote surface drainage away from sensitive areas and constructing perimeter berms or shallow interceptor trenches can help redirect any lateral subsurface flow.

Long-term performance of the site should be supported by soil moisture monitoring and, if necessary, the installation of shallow observation wells or lysimeters to detect early signs of lateral migration. Establishing and maintaining vegetative cover with deep-rooted plants will further enhance evapotranspiration and improve soil stability. These combined measures will help ensure that effluent remains within the treatment zone and does not pose a risk to nearby wells or surface water features.

## 2.10.6 Conclusion  
The local subsurface conditions in the Spangle area are consistent with the broader Columbia Plateau aquifer system, featuring an overburden aquifer underlain by layered basalt flows with variable hydraulic properties. Shallow fractured zones and thin overburden in some areas increase the vulnerability of the groundwater system to surface contamination. As such, effluent disposal design must incorporate hydrogeologic analysis and protective measures to safeguard both shallow domestic wells and deeper regional aquifers.

## 2.11 Population Growth and Projections  
WAC 173-240-050(3)(e)

---

The Spangle wastewater facility must be designed to accommodate future growth based on reliable population projections. Using a population projection that aligns with regional trends ensures that the facility is appropriately sized to meet demand without being overbuilt or inadequate. Selecting the right growth projection is essential for making efficient wastewater infrastructure investments, preventing both under-capacity issues and unnecessary spending on oversized facilities. The wastewater plan should integrate a multi-year progress reporting cycle to reassess capacity needs and funding allocations based on actual population trends. This approach ensures that financial resources are allocated effectively while supporting sustainable development in Spangle.

## 2.11.1 Population Data Sources
The population data for Spangle, Washington, was sourced from the Washington State Office of Financial Management (WAOFM), which produces annual population estimates based on the U.S. Census and other demographic data. These estimates include information on births, deaths, and migration patterns, as well as housing unit data. The WAOFM uses these sources to calculate population changes and trends, which are crucial for resource allocation and planning at the state and local levels. The data for Spangle, including the 2024 estimate of 285 residents, was released in July 2024 and reflects the most current population trends.

## 2.11.2 Demographic and Housing Analysis (2014–2024)
Over the decade from 2014 to 2024, Spangle’s population remained relatively stable, fluctuating between 272 and 285 residents. While the population showed minor fluctuations, it increased slightly from 279 in 2014 to 285 in 2024, reflecting modest growth over the period.

The number of total housing units increased gradually, from 127 in 2014 to 130 in 2024, indicating a slow but steady expansion of housing in response to population trends. Occupied housing units also followed an upward trend, increasing from 120 in 2014 to 125 in 2024, reflecting a stable demand for housing in the area.

The occupancy rate saw a steady increase during this period, starting at 94.49% in 2014 and reaching 96.92% in 2024. This rise in occupancy rate demonstrates a continued demand for housing and a high level of housing utilization.

In terms of average household size, there was a gradual decline from 2.33 people per household in 2014 to 2.28 in 2020. By 2024, the average household size remained relatively stable at 2.28, which may suggest a trend towards smaller households or fewer people per home.

Overall, Spangle experienced a decade of modest population growth, stable housing demand, and a slight decrease in household size. The steady increase in occupancy rates and housing units over the years reflects a well-settled community with a steady moderate demand for housing, while the decline in average household size is indicative of broader demographic shifts.

---

### 2.11.3 Comparison of Demographic and Housing Trends: Spokane County vs. Spangle

Spokane County and Spangle exhibit some key demographic and housing similarities, but also notable differences. Over the decade from 2014 to 2024, Spokane County’s population steadily grew from 471,221 to 539,339, reflecting a broader trend of population increase in metropolitan areas driven by both natural change and immigration. In contrast, Spangle’s population remained relatively stable, with slight fluctuations between 272 and 280 residents over the same period, indicating a more modest and localized growth.

Both areas saw increases in housing stock during this period, with Spokane County’s total housing units rising from 201,434 in 2014 to 224,019 in 2024, while Spangle saw a more gradual increase from 127 to 130 housing units. Occupancy rates in both areas demonstrated strong housing demand, with Spokane County’s rate climbing from 92.92% in 2014 to 94.84% in 2024, and Spangle’s occupancy rate increasing from 94.49% in 2014 to 96.92% in 2024.

Overall, while Spokane County’s growth reflects a broader trend of urbanization, in Spangle, the steady but slower population and housing growth suggests a more stable, less dynamic community. Nonetheless, both areas experienced consistent demand for housing, with rising occupancy rates indicating strong housing utilization in both regions.

By 2024, the population had grown to 285, with the land area increasing to 0.35 square miles. This expansion in the land area likely reflects annexations or boundary adjustments,

---

In 1980, the population density was 1,062 people per square mile. By 2024, with a population of 285 and a land area of 0.35 square miles, the population density has decreased to approximately 815.5 people per square mile. This trend suggests that while the population has grown, the city has also expanded its boundaries, leading to a decrease in population density over time.

## 2.12 Population Projection Methodology  
To support long-term infrastructure planning, population projections for the Town of Spangle were developed using historical data from 1980 to 2024. The projections extend over a 6-year period (2031) and a 20-year period (2045) to assist in facility capacity planning, particularly for wastewater treatment and sewer conveyance infrastructure.

### 2.12.1 Data Sources  
The analysis utilized historical population figures from the U.S. Census and the Washington State Office of Financial Management (WAOFM). These datasets provide insights into past population

---

trends, city limit area changes, and population density variations. Additional considerations included housing occupancy rates, total housing units, and average household size, all of which influence population dynamics.

To estimate future growth, two distinct forecasting methods were employed:

## 2.12.2 Method 1: Population Trend Analysis
To project future population trends, historical population data for Spangle was analyzed using Microsoft Excel. A scatter plot was created, and a polynomial trendline was applied to capture complex, non-linear growth patterns over time. Unlike a simple linear regression, this trendline accommodates fluctuations and varying growth rates, providing a more accurate representation of population changes.

The best-fit polynomial equation derived from the trendline was then used to forecast future population values. By substituting the target years (e.g., 2031 and 2045) into the equation, population projections were generated. This method offers a data-driven approach to long-term planning, ensuring that future trends are estimated based on observed historical patterns rather than assuming a constant rate of growth.

## 2.12.3 Method 2: Housing Unit-Based Projection

The second method estimates future population based on housing data. It considers the number of total housing units, occupancy rates, and average household size to determine population projections. The formula used is:

$$
\text{Projected Population} = \text{Total Housing Units} \times \text{Occupancy Rate} \times \text{Average Household Size}
$$

By analyzing historical trends in housing unit growth and occupancy rates, future values are estimated, and the formula is applied to project population growth. This method links population trends to housing availability and household size, making it a valuable tool for infrastructure planning and residential development assessments.

To estimate the number of future housing units, historical data from the Office of Financial Management, covering the period from 1980 to the present year (2024), as well as Wheatland housing development design documents, has been used.

The Wheatland Housing Development is a new residential subdivision within the city limits of Spangle, Washington, planned to span approximately 50.55 acres and create 190 residential lots. The project is bordered by Highway 195 to the west, Old Highway 195 and existing residential and commercial to the east, the wastewater treatment facility to the north, and Cheney-Spangle Highway to the south. The development will cover three parcels, identified as 32045.0044, 32042.0038, and 32042.0046, located within W ½ Section 4, Township 22 North, Range 43 East, W.M. Designed to accommodate the future growth of Spangle, the subdivision will provide a well-planned, residential community that complements the surrounding land use.

---

The same forecasting method applied to population projections was also used for housing unit projections. Based on this approach, housing estimates were generated for the years 2031 and 2045.

For calculating the number of housing units over 6-year and 20-year planning periods, two scenarios were considered:

**Scenario 1 – Organic Growth:**
* Assumes no major housing developments will occur.
* Spangle continues to grow naturally, following historical trends.

**Scenario 2 – Wheatland Estates Development:**
* Assumes the proposed Wheatland Estates housing development will be completed within the planning period.
* This development significantly increases the number of housing units and subsequently impacts population growth.

The housing development in Scenario 2 was assumed to take place in three phases, starting in 2027, with approximately one-third of the total housing units completed and occupied every six years. The projected impact of both scenarios is illustrated in Chart 2.2.

## 2.12.4 Projected Population Using Housing Unit-Based Forecasting

**Scenario 1: Organic Growth (Without Wheatland Estates Development)**

The first scenario assumes that the Wheatland Estates development does not proceed, and Spangle continues to grow according to its natural, socio-economic development trends. In this case, the projected population growth is based purely on historical housing data, occupancy rates, and average household sizes. For 2031, the forecasted population is approximately 297 people, and by 2045, it is projected to be around 330 people.

**Scenario 2: Wheatland Estates Development**

The second scenario assumes that the Wheatland Estates housing development is completed within the planning periods, significantly increasing the number of housing units in Spangle. The

## 2.13 Summary and Conclusion

The population projection study for the Town of Spangle utilized two forecasting methods to support long-term infrastructure planning. The first method analyzed historical population trends, applying a polynomial trendline to capture past growth patterns and project future trends. This approach provides a data-driven estimate of future population based on observed demographic shifts over time.

The second method relied on housing unit data, considering occupancy rates and household sizes to estimate population growth. Within this method, two scenarios were explored: one assuming natural socio-economic development without major housing projects and another factoring in the planned development of the Wheatland Estates. The first scenario follows historical housing trends, reflecting steady organic growth, while the second scenario accounts for an accelerated increase in housing availability, leading to a more significant rise in projected population.

## 2.14 Conclusion

The analysis highlights how different forecasting approaches can yield varying population projections, demonstrating the impact of housing development on future growth. The population trend analysis captures long-term patterns based on historical data, while the housing unit-based projection highlights the direct influence of residential expansion and socio-economic factors, such as aging populations, economic cycles, and unexpected events like the COVID-19 pandemic. The findings emphasize the importance of considering both organic growth trends and planned developments when planning infrastructure and public services. By integrating these projections, decision-makers can ensure that future growth is managed effectively, supporting sustainable development and adequate resource allocation.

# Chapter 3: Regulatory Framework And Compliance For Wastewater Planning

### 3.1 Introduction

This chapter outlines the regulatory framework and key considerations that influence wastewater discharge planning. Specifically, it evaluates two discharge alternatives considered in the facility plan: surface water discharge to Spangle Creek and land application through irrigation. This chapter focuses on the primary federal regulation governing wastewater treatment—the National Pollutant Discharge Elimination System (NPDES) permit—which sets the compliance requirements for surface water discharges. The chapter also briefly addresses other relevant federal and state regulations and programs that affect effluent standards for land application and biosolids management, with further details on biosolids, nitrogen levels, and related topics provided in Chapter 7.

### 3.2 Federal and State Regulations

WAC 173-240-060(4)(d)-(f)

The National Pollutant Discharge Elimination System (NPDES) program, established under Section 402 of the CWA, regulates wastewater discharges by setting pollutant limits. In

---

Washington State, the Department of Ecology administers NPDES permits on behalf of the U.S. Environmental Protection Agency (EPA). These permits define the allowable quantity and quality of wastewater discharges to protect water resources.

In general, any wastewater discharge to surface waters requires an NPDES permit, while discharges via land application require a state wastewater permit.

Washington State regulations provide detailed procedures for:

* Technical criteria for municipal wastewater discharges (WAC 173-221)  
* Water quality standards for surface and groundwater (WAC 173-201A and WAC 173-200)  
* Sediment management standards (WAC 173-204)  

These regulations form the basis of the NPDES permitting system, establishing effluent limitations and other permit requirements.

The Town of Spangle’s sewage treatment plant is required to obtain an individual NPDES permit from the Washington State Department of Ecology for wastewater discharges to Spangle Creek. This permit sets specific limits on the type and amount of pollutants that can be discharged into the creek to ensure compliance with state water quality standards. It also includes provisions for monitoring and reporting to confirm that the plant is meeting these effluent limits.

### 3.3 Technical criteria for municipal wastewater discharges  
WAC 173-240-060(3)(d)

The following table summarizes the effluent discharge standards for domestic wastewater facilities that release effluent into surface waters, as established by WAC 173-221-040. These limits regulate BOD, TSS, fecal coliform, and pH levels to ensure compliance with state water quality standards and environmental protection requirements.

The standards in Chapter 173-200 WAC outline the maximum allowable concentrations of pollutants in groundwater to ensure that the water remains safe for use and does not harm the ecosystem. The chapter also provides a framework for managing groundwater pollution, including the identification of significant sources of contamination and measures to reduce pollutant levels in groundwater.

### 3.4 Spangle Creek Water Quality and Designated Uses  
*WAC 173-240-060(3)(e)*

Spangle Creek, an intermittent stream located within the Hangman Creek watershed, has been the subject of ongoing environmental assessment due to concerns regarding water quality and the impact of wastewater discharges. As per the regulations set forth in Chapter 173-201A WAC, designated uses for Spangle Creek include the protection of aquatic life and recreational uses, both of which are subject to specific water quality criteria.

Spangle Creek serves multiple designated purposes, including support for aquatic life and recreational uses. The aquatic life uses are intended to protect both fish and non-fish species in the creek. Specific criteria have been set to safeguard these uses, particularly for salmonid spawning, rearing, and migration. The temperature criterion for salmonid spawning is set at a highest 7-day average daily maximum of 17.5°C. Dissolved oxygen levels are required to meet a minimum of 8.0 mg/L over a one-day period, ensuring adequate oxygen for aquatic organisms. Additionally, turbidity must not exceed 5 NTU over background levels when the background is 50 NTU or less, and a 10 percent increase in turbidity is allowed when background levels are above 50 NTU. The creek's pH should remain within a range of 6.5 to 8.5, with a variation of no more than 0.5 units due to human activities.

For recreational uses, the creek is classified under extraordinary primary contact recreation, primary contact recreation, and secondary contact recreation. In line with these classifications, fecal coliform levels in the creek must not exceed a geometric mean of 100 colonies per 100 mL, with no more than 10 percent of the samples exceeding 200 colonies per 100 mL.

#### 3.4.1 Current Water Quality Conditions  
Spangle Creek has experienced significant water quality degradation, especially during the summer months when the creek becomes relatively stagnant. As a result, Spangle Creek is currently listed on the 303(d) list as impaired for dissolved oxygen. This stagnant water condition

---

is exacerbated by a shallow wetland water table sustained by the Upper Columbia Academy and extending about a mile downstream of the Spangle wastewater treatment plant (WWTP) outfall. The water quality during these periods does not meet the established water quality standards, especially as the creek has limited flow and supports only a few species, primarily reptiles, amphibians, and arthropods.

Agricultural sediments significantly degrade the water quality when the creek is flowing, while nutrient releases from wetland vegetation contribute to further degradation when the creek slows to intermittent pools during the low-flow period. The creek’s current water quality is far below the desired standards, and a more comprehensive approach is required to address these deficiencies.

### 3.5 Receiving Water Study and Total Maximum Daily Load (TMDL)  
WAC 173-240-060(3)(e)

To better understand the impacts of the wastewater discharges on Spangle Creek, the proposed permit requires the Town of Spangle to conduct a receiving water study. This study will measure temperature and phosphorus concentrations both in the effluent and the receiving water to evaluate any downstream impacts. The study will also help provide additional data to supplement the Total Maximum Daily Load (TMDL) efforts for Hangman Creek, which includes Spangle Creek as a tributary.

A Total Maximum Daily Load (TMDL) sets the highest amount of a particular pollutant that a water body can safely absorb while still meeting its water quality standards. TMDLs typically focus on pollutants like oxygen-demanding substances, but can also address a variety of other pollutants such as temperature, pH, total suspended solids (TSS), and phosphorus.

Ecology has completed a TMDL for the Hangman (Latah) Creek Watershed, which specifically addresses fecal coliform bacteria, temperature, and turbidity. As part of this, Ecology established a Waste Load Allocation (WLA) for certain pollutants, including total suspended solids (TSS).

### 3.6 Spangle Creek TMDL Status and Future Considerations  
WAC 173-240-060(3)(e)

Spangle Creek currently does not have a specific Total Maximum Daily Load (TMDL) for dissolved oxygen (DO), though it is listed on the 303(d) list as impaired for this parameter. While there is no dedicated TMDL for Spangle Creek's DO levels, the creek is part of the Hangman Creek watershed, which is undergoing the development of an Advanced Restoration Plan (ARP) by Ecology. The ARP focuses on addressing nonpoint source pollution in the watershed, and although it includes Spangle Creek, it does not provide wasteload allocations. If the ARP does not successfully meet the dissolved oxygen criteria for Spangle Creek, wasteload allocations may be introduced in a future TMDL. Additionally, the Hangman Creek Watershed Fecal Coliform, Temperature, and Turbidity TMDL, published in 2011, includes wasteload allocations for the Spangle wastewater treatment plant for fecal coliform, temperature, and total suspended solids, but it does not yet address

---

dissolved oxygen specifically. The ongoing efforts to manage water quality in Spangle Creek will be supplemented by the ARP and potential future TMDL processes if necessary.

### 3.6.1 Temperature Compliance  
WAC 173-240-060(3)(e)

The temperature limits for Spangle Creek, based on the completed temperature Total Maximum Daily Load (TMDL) for the Hangman (Latah) Creek Watershed, are as follows:

June: 18.2°C

July: 21.5°C

August: 17.7°C

These limits are set to protect aquatic life and ensure compliance with water quality standards. The proposed permit includes a compliance schedule to meet these temperature limits, and Spangle is required to collect continuous temperature data to report the maximum daily temperature. This data will help develop a performance-based limit in future permits. In the interim, a narrative temperature limit is included, requiring Spangle to implement best management practices to minimize temperature increases.

### 3.6.2 Biochemical Oxygen Demand (BOD5) Monitoring and Compliance  
WAC 173-240-060(3)(d)

Until the effectiveness of water quality improvements is determined, the effluent discharged into Spangle Creek from the wastewater treatment plant must comply with both the current and proposed permit requirements. The treatment plant must meet limits of 10.0 mg/L equivalent to 5.7 lbs/day loading, and an 85% removal rate for influent BOD₅. Additionally, steps shall be taken to ensure that the plant achieves compliance with the state water quality standard for dissolved oxygen (DO), which requires a minimum concentration of 8.0 mg/L at the point of discharge. Due to a lack of representative daily readings, Ecology could not establish a strict performance-based limit in this permit cycle. To address this, continuous monitoring of dissolved oxygen levels is necessary, along with regular reporting of the lowest daily readings. This data will help in determining whether additional measures are required to ensure compliance with water quality standards.

### 3.6.3 Total Suspended Solids (TSS) Compliance  
WAC 173-240-060(3)(d)

The Spangle Wastewater Treatment Plant must adhere to established effluent limits for total suspended solids (TSS) to protect water quality in Spangle Creek. These limits are derived from the Total Maximum Daily Load (TMDL) for the Hangman Creek Watershed and are set to control the amount of suspended particles discharged into the waterway. The plant is required to meet an average monthly TSS limit of 15 mg/L and 8.5 lbs/day, along with an average weekly limit of 22.5 mg/L and 12.8 lbs/day.

---

### 3.6.4 Phosphorus (P) Monitoring and Compliance  
WAC 173-240-060(3)(d)

In Washington State, specific numeric water quality criteria for phosphorus are not established in Chapter 173-201A WAC. Instead, the state employs narrative criteria to manage nutrient pollution, including phosphorus, to prevent issues like eutrophication in water bodies. For instance, the Spokane River Dissolved Oxygen Total Maximum Daily Load (TMDL) process has set a phosphorus discharge target of 10 mg/L for municipal wastewater treatment plants to mitigate excessive algal growth that depletes dissolved oxygen levels.

In the Hangman Creek watershed, which includes Spangle Creek, phosphorus levels are a significant concern due to their contribution to downstream water quality issues. The Washington State Department of Ecology has identified the Hangman Watershed as the largest source of phosphorus to the Spokane River, with problems such as streambank erosion, loss of riparian vegetation, agricultural practices, and stormwater runoff exacerbating the issue.

To address these concerns, the Department of Ecology is developing an Advanced Restoration Plan (ARP) for the Hangman Creek watershed. This plan aims to implement best management practices and restoration activities to reduce phosphorus and sediment inputs, thereby improving water quality in both Hangman Creek and its tributaries, including Spangle Creek.

Until the results of the Advanced Restoration Plan (ARP) are finalized, and any potential Total Maximum Daily Load (TMDL) requirements are determined, the treatment plant must adhere to the current phosphorus limits. The performance-based phosphorus limits are intended to maintain phosphorus inputs at current levels until nonpoint sources are addressed and the point source effects can be identified.

The performance-based phosphorus limits are as follows:

* Maximum Daily Effluent Limit: 10.8 mg/L  
* Average Monthly Effluent Limit: 5.7 mg/L  

### 3.6.5 Ammonia Monitoring and Compliance  
WAC 173-240-060(3)(d)

Ammonia is a known toxic pollutant in the Spangle Wastewater Treatment Plant's effluent, and its potential to exceed water quality standards must be evaluated. The toxicity of ammonia depends on the proportion of its unionized form, which varies with temperature and pH in the receiving water. Since no valid background data on ammonia levels in Spangle Creek is available, Ecology assumed a background concentration of zero. To address this data gap, Spangle is required to monitor and collect ammonia concentration data upstream of the discharge point. The results of this monitoring could lead to future permit modifications or additional effluent limits to ensure compliance with water quality standards and protect aquatic life.

### 3.6.6 Bacteria Compliance and Monitoring Requirements  
WAC 173-240-060(3)(d)

---

Ecology has completed a Total Maximum Daily Load (TMDL) for the Hangman (Latah) Creek Watershed, which includes fecal coliform bacteria, temperature, and turbidity. The established wasteload allocation (WLA) for the Spangle discharge is 6.6 × 10^8 cfu/day. However, the previous permit's fecal coliform limit of 100 cfu/100 mL was more stringent, and as a result, the proposed permit will continue to enforce the previous technology-based fecal coliform limit of 100 cfu/100 mL.

Ecology will not require the use of E. coli as an alternative indicator at 100 cfu/100 mL. However, Ecology will require sampling to assess the potential for exceeding the E. coli criteria in the next permit period.

### 3.6.7 Compliance with Human Health-Based Water Quality Criteria  
WAC 173-240-060(3)(e)

Washington’s water quality standards include numeric human health-based criteria for priority pollutants. In this case, it has been determined that there are no industrial discharges, which makes it unlikely that the discharge contains chemicals that could pose a risk to human health. As a result, the water quality standards for human health-based criteria are not a concern for this particular discharge.

### 3.6.8 Sediment Quality and Standards Compliance  
WAC 173-240-060(3)(e)

The aquatic sediment standards, outlined in chapter 173-204 WAC, are designed to protect aquatic life and human health. These standards allow Ecology to assess whether a discharge could potentially violate sediment quality standards (WAC 173-204-400).[^1]

After reviewing both the discharge and effluent characteristics, Ecology has determined that the discharge does not pose a reasonable risk of violating sediment management standards. Specifically, toxics are not accumulating in the sediments, even though the point of discharge is located in a depositional area. Therefore, sediment quality is not a concern in this case.

[^1]: Further information on sediment standards can be found on the Aquatic Lands Cleanup Unit website.  
Sediment cleanups - Washington State Department of Ecology

### 3.6.9 Groundwater Quality Compliance and Protection  
WAC 173-240-060(4)

Groundwater quality standards (Chapter 173-200 WAC) are established to protect the beneficial uses of groundwater. Any discharge of pollutants must comply with these standards to prevent groundwater pollution and degradation.

Currently, waste activated sludge is being discharged into an old, unlined lagoon, which may impact groundwater quality. To ensure compliance, facilities must demonstrate that they are implementing all known, available, and reasonable methods of prevention, control, and treatment (AKART) as required under WAC 173-200-050. Additionally, facilities must establish

---

enforcement limits that do not exceed groundwater quality criteria, except under specific conditions outlined in WAC 173-200-050(3).

### 3.6.10 Groundwater Toxicity Compliance and Monitoring Standards  
WAC 173-240-060(4)  

The water quality standards for groundwater prohibit the discharge of pollutants that could harm the quality of the receiving groundwater. Many hazardous pollutants are not easily measurable by conventional detection methods. However, toxicity can be assessed by using laboratory tests that expose living organisms to the discharge and observe their responses. These tests evaluate the overall toxicity of the effluent, which is known as whole effluent toxicity (WET) testing. Some WET tests focus on acute toxicity, while others measure chronic toxicity. Based on the screening criteria in Chapter 173-200-050 WAC, Ecology has determined that toxic effects from unidentified pollutants in the groundwater are unlikely. However, Ecology may mandate WET testing if new information suggests the presence of toxicity in the effluent."

### 3.6.11 PH Compliance and Monitoring Requirements  
WAC 173-240-060(3)(d)  

Ecology currently lacks the data needed to model the impact of the effluent pH on the receiving water. However, based on available information, Ecology predicts that the effluent will not violate pH criteria under critical conditions. To confirm this, the facility is required to gather the necessary data given that the facility has demonstrated its ability to meet water quality standards, the pH limits for the effluent remain within the range of 6.5 to 8.5.

### 3.7 Technology-Based Effluent Limits  
WAC 173-240-060(3)(f)  

Federal and state regulations define technology-based effluent limits for domestic wastewater treatment plants. These limits are outlined in 40 CFR Part 133 (federal) and Chapter 173-221 WAC (state). According to Chapter 173-220-130 WAC, "effluent limitations shall not be less stringent than those based upon the treatment facility design efficiency contained in approved engineering plans and reports.

### 3.8 Proposed Effluent limits  
*WAC 173-240-060(4)(d)-(f)*

To ensure long-term compliance with both state and federal water quality regulations, the Town of Spangle’s wastewater treatment system will adopt the strictest effluent limits from either technology-based or water quality-based standards. This proactive approach guarantees that future regulatory changes will not necessitate costly upgrades, while also maintaining the highest environmental protection standards for receiving water bodies. The proposed limits, shown in Table 3.3, are derived from the most stringent applicable criteria, ensuring minimal pollutant discharge and promoting sustainable water resource management.

### 3.9 Regulatory Requirements for Land Application of Wastewater Effluent  
*WAC 173-240-060(4)*

The land application of treated municipal wastewater is also subjected to federal and state regulatory requirements designed to protect public health and the environment.

At the federal level, land application systems must comply with the Clean Water Act (CWA), administered by the U.S. Environmental Protection Agency (EPA). Although land application is considered a beneficial method of wastewater reuse, it may still fall under the National Pollutant Discharge Elimination System (NPDES) permitting requirements if there is a potential for discharge to surface waters. If no direct discharge occurs, an NPDES permit may not be required; however, effluent limits for nutrients and pathogens still apply under EPA guidance, particularly the *Process Design Manual for Land Treatment of Municipal Wastewater Effluents* (2006).

At the state level, the Washington State Department of Ecology (Ecology) is the primary regulatory agency for wastewater reuse. Land application systems in Washington are regulated under Chapter 173-216 WAC (State Waste Discharge Permit Program) and Chapter 173-200 WAC (Groundwater Quality Standards). A State Waste Discharge Permit is required for land treatment projects, and systems must demonstrate compliance with groundwater quality standards. Regulatory oversight includes detailed assessments of nitrogen loading, pathogen reduction, and trace metals to prevent contamination of underlying aquifers.

Additionally, if the treated effluent meets the criteria for reclaimed water, Washington’s Reclaimed Water Use Act (RCW 90.46) may apply. Biosolids generated at the treatment facility are

---

expected to be stabilized and treated in accordance with WAC 173-308 and 40 CFR Part 503,  
allowing for agricultural land application.

For more information on regulatory requirements and standards for wastewater effluent and  
biosolids treatment quality criteria, refer to Chapter 7.

---

# Table Regulatory Framework And Compliance For Wastewater Planning.9: Treatment Standards and Proposed Effluent Limits Summary

<table>

    <tr>
        <th>Parameter</th>

(mg/l)</th>
        <th colspan="2">Technology Based Limit

---

### 3.10 Flow Measurement and Monitoring  
In accordance with NPDES Permit WA0991010, the Town of Spangle should install a continuous totalizing flow meter at both influent and effluent and carry out regular monitoring of BOD₅, TSS, ammonia, total phosphorus, fecal coliform, pH, temperature, and dissolved oxygen to help ensure compliance with permit limits, load calculations, and reporting requirements (WAC 173-240-060(3)(c)).

### 3.11 Conclusion  
In conclusion, Spangle Creek is facing significant water quality challenges, particularly during the summer months when the creek experiences stagnant conditions and degraded water quality due to agricultural runoff and nutrient release from wetlands. The ongoing receiving water study and the TMDL process will provide critical data to help guide efforts to improve the water quality in the creek. These efforts, along with stricter water quality standards for temperature, fecal coliform, turbidity, and pH, aim to bring Spangle Creek into compliance with state and federal regulations, ensuring the protection of aquatic life and recreational uses for the future.

---

# Chapter 4: Existing Sewer System

## 4.1 Introduction  
This chapter provides an overview of the existing facilities that comprise the wastewater collection, treatment, and disposal systems for the Town of Spangle. These facilities include gravity sewers, a pump station, force mains, wastewater treatment facilities, and effluent and biosolids disposal facilities.

## 4.2 Collection System Overview  
*WAC 173-240-050(3)(d)(ii)*

The wastewater collection system in Spangle was constructed in 1977 to serve an initial population of 200. By 2024, the town’s population had grown to 285. Despite this growth, the original system has remained largely unchanged since its construction.

The collection system consists of gravity sewers, a lift station, force main and an effluent discharge system.

* Gravity Sewers: The system uses 8 and 6 inch PVC sewer pipes to convey wastewater from the service areas to a lift station located at the north end of town. With the exception of two instances, all sewer pipes meet or exceed the minimum required slope.

* Lift Station: The lift station is equipped with two 7.5-horsepower pumps and a wet well to manage wastewater flow.

* Force Main: From the lift station, wastewater is transported approximately 2200 feet through a 4-inch pressure main to the headworks of the wastewater treatment facility.

* Effluent Discharge:  
  The treated and disinfected effluent is discharged into Spangle Creek through a 750-foot discharge pipe, terminating with a flap gate at the creek’s edge, located just north of the town and east of the wastewater treatment facility.

The existing sanitary sewer system is illustrated in Figure 4.1, and a summary of the gravity sewer pipes, and force mains is provided in Table 4.1. As shown in Figure 4.1, the existing service boundary is inside the city limit. The proposed new housing development discussed in chapter 4 also is located inside the city limit.

# 4.3 Service Area  
WAC 173-240-050(3)(c)  

The town's corporate limits encompass a total area of 225 acres. The sanitary sewer collection system serves approximately 105 acres within the service area located inside the city limits. This service area is divided into five collection areas, referred to as "sewersheds." These sewersheds are delineated based on the topography of the terrain and the final discharge point, which is the lift station wet well. (See Figure 4.2 for the sewersheds map)  

Since stormwater is collected and discharged separately from the sanitary sewer system, no contributing stormwater area or drainage pattern is assigned or incorporated in the mapping of these sewersheds.  

# 4.4 Lift Station  
WAC 173-240-050(3)(d)(iv)  

Spangle has one lift station within its sanitary sewer system, located as shown in Figure 4.1. This pump station pumps sewage from all five service areas via a 4-inch ductile iron pipe force main installed along Old SR 95 to the treatment plant. # 4.5 Wastewater Treatment Facility  
WAC 173-240-050(3)(h)  

## 4.5.1 Background  
The Town of Spangle operates the Town of Spangle Wastewater Treatment Plant (WWTP), a facility utilizing the Biolac treatment system. Specifically, the plant employs the Extended Aeration Activated Sludge System, which incorporates denitrifying activated sludge processes. This system is designed to efficiently treat wastewater by promoting the biological degradation of organic pollutants and reducing nitrogen compounds.  

## 4.5.2 History  
In 1993, the Town of Spangle submitted a Facility Plan to the Washington State Department of Ecology (Ecology). The plan proposed adding a third 5-acre lagoon and a 20-acre spray irrigation field to the treatment facility. However, due to changes in local conditions and site availability, the Town was unable to implement these recommendations.  

In 1995, Ecology developed a new policy for intermittent streams, which made the discharge of treated wastewater into Spangle Creek a viable option for the Town. This policy shift provided an opportunity for the Town to upgrade its wastewater treatment facility. As a result, in 1996, the Town completed an addendum to the original Facility Plan, which was subsequently approved by Ecology. The final recommended treatment solution was the current Extended Aeration Activated Sludge Process (Biolac System), which remains in use today.  

## 4.5.3 Facility Discharge  
The wastewater treatment plant discharges its treated effluent into Spangle Creek, located east of the treatment plant. The discharge point coordinates are Latitude 47º 26' 04" N and Longitude 117º 22' 54" W.  

## 4.5.4 Regulatory Oversight  
The WWTP operates under the oversight of regulatory agencies, including maintaining an NPDES (National Pollutant Discharge Elimination System) permit. This permit ensures that the plant’s discharge complies with water quality criteria set by both state and federal regulations, thereby helping to protect the quality of Spangle Creek’s water and safeguard aquatic ecosystems and public health.  

**Waterbody ID Number:** 1173418475011  

---

# 4.5.5 Treatment Processes

The Town of Spangle Wastewater Treatment Plant (WWTP) employs several treatment processes to ensure the effective treatment of wastewater before it is discharged into Spangle Creek.

The treatment system includes:

**Headworks – Mechanically Cleaned Bar Screen:** The bar screen is designed to remove large debris from incoming wastewater before it undergoes further treatment. According to the Town, there is no bypass system from the headworks or the lift station to the old lagoon. The headworks is equipped with a grit chamber and a self-cleaning screen. There are two sources of flow entering the headworks: one is scum overflow from the secondary clarifier, and the other comes from the lift station in the collection system. The composite sampler is located after the bar screen, allowing it to collect samples that include both scum from the secondary clarifier and raw sewage from the incoming flow.

**Lined Earthen Aeration Basin:** The primary treatment process is a Biolac system that utilizes aeration chains suspended from floats in a lined earthen basin. The Biolac system is highly effective across various operating conditions (F/M ratios) and hydraulic loadings due to the large volume of the aeration basin. The aeration chains provide efficient oxygen mixing, which promotes effective nitrification. Additionally, the chains create anoxic zones that are capable of denitrifying most of the nitrate present in the wastewater.

**Secondary Clarifier:** After aeration, the wastewater moves to a secondary clarifier, which allows for the separation of solids from the treated effluent.

Following the secondary treatment process, the effluent undergoes ultraviolet (UV) disinfection before being discharged into Spangle Creek.

The system includes an integral clarifier that separates and recycles biological solids. Return activated sludge (RAS) is airlifted from the bottom of the clarifier and sent back to the influent pipe of the aeration basin, while a portion of the sludge is removed as waste activated sludge (WAS). The WAS is directed to further treatment in the biosolids drying pond (old lagoon). The facility has a biosolids permit, number BA0045471. In 2015, Spangle received approval for the design of an aerobic digester, which was constructed in 2016; however, it has not yet been brought online due to operational issues. To improve sludge management, the Town could prioritize repairing and commissioning the aerobic digester to stabilize the sludge. The Town has also purchased a belt filter press for dewatering, which will be used once a loading facility and storage vault for untreated sludge are constructed. Once operational, these improvements would allow sludge to be properly stabilized, dewatered, and safely stored, reducing reliance on the old lagoon and improving overall plant efficiency.

The Town of Spangle's wastewater treatment plant (WWTP) discharges treated water into Spangle Creek, which is affected by various pollution sources. While upstream direct discharges have stopped due to the Upper Columbia Academy's wetland treatment system, significant indirect pollution sources remain. These include agricultural runoff, the Town's stormwater

---

system, and nutrient pollution from the Upper Columbia Academy's wetland, pavement, and lawn care practices. Together, these factors impact the water quality of Spangle Creek.

There is no existing data on the water quality or condition for this section of Spangle Creek because the creek doesn't flow all the time in this area.

The treatment facility requires a certified Class II operator to manage its operations and maintain compliance with regulatory requirements.

Under WAC 173-220-150 (1)(g), the Spangle Wastewater Treatment Plant is required to properly operate and maintain its facilities at all times to comply with the terms and conditions of the issued permit. It is essential that flows and waste loadings do not exceed the approved design criteria or any approved revisions.

## 4.6 Effluent Limitations  
*WAC 173-240-060(3)(c)*

The Town of Spangle Wastewater Treatment Plant (WWTP) is required to follow all terms and conditions outlined in its current permit issued by the Washington Department of Ecology. Permit WA0991010 was issued on February 12, 2025, became effective on April 1, 2025, and will expire on March 31, 2030. The permit sets limits for discharges, including final limits for dissolved oxygen and Total Maximum Daily Load (TMDL) limits for temperature.

The effluent limits for the City’s treatment plant, as established in both the previous and current proposed NPDES permits, are based on the most stringent requirements of the TMDL and technology-based limits if parameters based on water quality limits are defined. For parameters not addressed by water quality criteria, technology-based effluent limits apply. These limits are shown in Table 3.3.

Discharging pollutants beyond the authorized limits or more frequently than permitted is considered a violation of the permit conditions.

The permit includes specific performance-based limits for total phosphorus to ensure that phosphorus concentrations in the discharged wastewater remain at current levels. This will

---

prevent an increase in the total phosphorus discharged, allowing Ecology time to address and reduce other sources of pollution in the surrounding areas that contribute to the water quality of Spangle Creek and its connected waterways.

From the effective date of the permit until its expiration, the Town of Spangle is authorized to discharge municipal wastewater, subject to compliance with the following terms and conditions

**Explanation of Effluent Limitations:**  
Average Monthly and Weekly Limitations: These limits represent the average concentrations of pollutants in the effluent, based on samples taken during a specified period. For fecal coliform, the average monthly and weekly limits are expressed as the geometric mean of the samples taken during that period.

Maximum Daily Limitation: This limit refers to the highest allowable concentration of a pollutant in the discharge on any given day. For pollutants measured in mass (e.g., BOD₅ and TSS), this is calculated as the total mass of the pollutant discharged in a 24-hour period.

Ecology-Based More Stringent Concentration Limits for Spangle WWTP

The above concentration limits for BOD₅, TSS, and fecal coliform were established based on Ecology's review of the Town of Spangle WWTP's design criteria. Specifically, Ecology applied stricter concentration limits than the federal or state baseline regulations, incorporating the performance expectations set by the engineering report prepared by Varela and Associates, Inc. in 1996. The Biolac system’s design, if properly operated, is expected to meet these more stringent limits for BOD₅, TSS, ammonia, and pH.

---

# 4.7 Plans for Maintaining Adequate Capacity

The Town of Spangle Wastewater Treatment Plant (WWTP) must develop and submit a plan, along with a schedule for maintaining sufficient capacity, to the Washington State Department of Ecology under the following conditions:

## 1. Flow or Waste Load Thresholds:

* Flow: The actual flow exceeds 85% of the design flow of 68,000 gallons per day, reaching 57,800 gallons/day, for three consecutive months.
* BOD₅ Loading: The actual BOD₅ waste load exceeds 85% of the design load of 137 lbs/day, reaching 117 lbs/day, for three consecutive months.
* TSS Loading: The actual TSS waste load exceeds 85% of the design load of 103 lbs/day, reaching 88 lbs/day, for three consecutive months.

## 2. Projected Increase in Flow or Waste Load:

* The projected increase is anticipated to reach the design capacity within five years.

WAC 173-240-060(3)(e)

Hangman Creek was used for this study because it is located in a similar watershed and environmental setting as Spangle Creek. Studies conducted on nearby or comparable water bodies can provide useful insights into water quality conditions that may also apply to Spangle Creek, particularly if they share similar sources of pollution, weather patterns, or other influencing factors. For the NPDES Permit (WA0991010), the data used for this permit comes from the Hangman Creek Dissolved Oxygen and pH TMDL Study (Study ID JJoy0005).
