# 4.9 Process Flow for Spangle WWTP

The following is the process flow for the Town of Spangle Wastewater Treatment Plant (WWTP). This treatment facility utilizes a Biolac extended aeration system designed to handle domestic wastewater from approximately 290 people, along with waste from a small restaurant and convenience store. The process involves multiple treatment stages, including headworks, aeration, clarification, disinfection, and final effluent discharge. Each of these steps plays a crucial role in ensuring that the wastewater meets required standards before being released into Spangle Creek. The process is designed to handle both regular and seasonal variations in wastewater flow, with the aim of achieving effective treatment and compliance with permit requirements. Below is a detailed outline of the treatment process flow at Spangle WWTP:

---

# 1. Incoming Flow:

Sources: Domestic wastewater from approximately 285 people, plus a small restaurant and convenience store.

# 2. Headworks:

* Components: Grit chamber and self-cleaning screen.  
* Flow Sources:  
  - Scum overflow from the secondary clarifier.  
  - Lift station flow from the collection system.

# 3. Cleanout:

* Function: After passing through the headworks, the flow is directed to a cleanout that includes a pipe from the recycle activated sludge.  
* Discharge: The cleanout discharges to the aeration basin.

# 4. Aeration Basin:

* Design: High-density polyethylene-lined earthen basin.  
* Process: The center portion of the basin receives air to aid in biological treatment.  
* Discharge: Gravity flow of treated wastewater proceeds to the secondary clarifier.

# 5. Secondary Clarifier:

Three Waste Streams:

* Scum Collection: The scum collector gathers treated wastewater and some solids from the surface and recycles the flow to the headworks via a lift pump.  
* Thickened Solids: Solids at the bottom of the clarifier are returned to the aeration basin as return activated sludge (RAS) or sent to the abandoned lagoon as waste activated sludge (WAS). (The aerobic digester, which is currently non-operational, was designed to handle WAS.)  
* Effluent: The clarified effluent enters a quiescent zone around the effluent weir and discharges to UV disinfection.

# 6. UV Disinfection:

* Design: The UV system includes a redundant bulb bank and a U-shaped tank. Flow enters on the north side, passing through both bulb banks, but only one set of bulbs is operated at a time. Bulbs are rotated periodically to ensure even wear.

# 7. Effluent Discharge:

* Effluent Flow: After UV disinfection, the treated effluent flows through an effluent flow gauge.

---

* Final Discharge: The treated water is discharged to Spangle Creek, approximately 750 feet east of the treatment facility.

**FIGURE EXISTING SEWER SYSTEM.11: SPANGLE WASTEWATER TREATMENT PLANT PROCESS DIAGRAM**

[Place holder]

This Photo by Unknown Author is licensed under CC BY-SA

---

# Chapter 5: Existing And Projected Wastewater Characteristics  
WAC 173-240-050(3)(h)(i)

## 5.1 Introduction  
The wastewater in the town of Spangle is primarily generated from domestic sources, mostly from residential units, with smaller contributions from a few commercial and public facilities, such as a church and restaurant. There are no industrial source or public facilities, such as schools or parks, contributing to the system. It is important to note that the sewer system is a separate system and is not intended to convey runoff generated from stormwater.

However, as is common in any sewer system, infiltration and inflow, resulting from groundwater and surface water entering the collection system during periods of high groundwater levels and rainfall, increase the overall sewer flow.

Wastewater characteristics are determined by flow patterns and chemical composition. In wet weather, increased infiltration and inflow can enter the collection system, which can significantly alter these characteristics. Data from the Spangle treatment plant, along with metered water demand, have been used to assess these characteristics. This information is crucial for both the initial and design years and is presented in the following sections.

This chapter aims to provide valuable data for evaluating wastewater characteristics, which will be used in the design of treatment facilities.

## 5.2 Wastewater Flow Characteristics: Terms and Application  
Exploring a variety of flows or combinations of flows and loads into the system is essential for simulating and evaluating the system’s performance under different times and conditions. The choice of flows and loads depends on the specific objective of the system assessment. The flows commonly used in hydraulic and treatment models are shown in table 5.1: Terms and Application

**Biochemical Oxygen Demand (BOD5):** Biochemical Oxygen Demand (BOD5) measures the amount of oxygen required by microorganisms to biologically oxidize organic matter in wastewater. It serves as a key indicator of wastewater strength and its potential impact on dissolved oxygen levels in receiving water bodies. If untreated BOD5 is discharged, biodegradable organics can deplete natural oxygen resources, leading to septic (anaerobic) conditions.

---

BOD5 data, along with other parameters, is essential for designing biological treatment facilities, determining organic loadings to treatment plants, and assessing the effectiveness of the treatment process. It also plays a crucial role in stream pollution control by evaluating the degree of organic pollution in water bodies.

---

<table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th>Parameters</th>
<th>Definition</th>
<th>Application</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td><b>Annual Average Design Flow (AADF) or Average Daily Flow</b></td>
<td>The average of the daily flow volumes anticipated to occur over a calendar year.</td>
<td>
* Used to evaluate treatment plant capacity and sizing many treatment units<br>
* Calculate organic ladings, estimate Sludge solids and chemical needs<br>
* Calculate pumping and treatment unit costs<br>
* Used as a base flow for peaking factors and pattern loads.<br>
* Helps estimate yearly operational and maintenance expenses for treatment and lift station systems.
</td>
</tr>

<td><b>Base Sanitary Flow (BSF)</b></td>

<td>
* Used for water quality analysis and load projections in areas without significant infiltration.

* Helps determine the impact of Inflow and Infiltration (I&I) on overall conveyance and treatment systems.<br>
* Guides design of treatment units like aeration tanks, sedimentation basins, clarifiers, and pumping stations.<br>
* Used to compare with Peak Wet Weather Flow (PWWF) to design for surges during storms or high-use events.
</td>

<i>*Tchobanoglous, G., Burton, F.L. and Stensel, H.D. (2003) Wastewater Engineering, Treatment and Reuse. 4th Edition, McGraw-Hill, Boston.</i>

---

BOD5 is traditionally measured as the amount of oxygen consumed over a five-day period at 20°C under aerobic conditions by a mixed population of microorganisms as they stabilize organic matter. It is typically expressed as a concentration in milligrams per liter (mg/L) or as a load in pounds per day (lb/d).

For the purpose of evaluating the adequacy of the existing conveyance system, a steady-state simulation is sufficient. The maximum flow loads developed and applied to the hydraulic model, which will be discussed further in the following sections.

**Total Suspended Solids (TSS):** Total Suspended Solids (TSS) refer to the suspended material in wastewater, which includes both organic and inorganic components such as sand, silt, clay, and organic matter. These solids are typically filterable, with a particle retention of 1.5 micrometers. The concentration of TSS in municipal wastewater typically averages around 230 mg/L.

The quantity of suspended solids removed during wastewater treatment varies depending on the type and level of treatment, which is essential for determining the sizing of mechanical and process units. When discharged into natural water bodies, TSS can increase water turbidity, negatively affecting aquatic life by disrupting spawning and breeding grounds. Additionally, organic solids that settle to the bottom decompose, consuming dissolved oxygen and producing noxious gases, further impacting aquatic ecosystems.

As a result, secondary effluent standards for wastewater treatment are often defined in terms of BOD₅ and TSS to ensure water quality is maintained and environmental impacts are minimized.

### 5.3 Wastewater Flow  
WAC 173-240-060(3)(c)

The total effluent to the wastewater treatment plant consists of untreated flow primarily from residential customers. The sewage generated at the city limit is entirely collected by a gravity collection system, which transports it to a lift station at the north end of town. The lift station is equipped with two 7.5 hp pumps and a wet well. The sewage is then pumped approximately 3,000 feet through a 4-inch pressure main to the wastewater treatment facility headworks. Since no sewage is reported to be disposed of on-site, it is assumed that all customers are connected to the system.

The average daily flow rate generated from each connection is estimated using the Wastewater Treatment Plant Discharge Monitoring Report (**DMR**) from 2018 through 2024. The estimated average flow to the treatment plant is summarized in the table below:

### 5.3.1 Estimating Average Daily  
The system is assessed based on the number of Equivalent Residential Units (ERUs) requiring sewer service. An ERU represents the average daily flow (ADF), measured in gallons per day (gpd), from a typical single-family home. According to the chart 2.2, a typical single-family home in the town houses an average of 2.28 people.

The estimated average daily flow (ADF), as shown in the above table, ranges between 74 and 90 gpd. This flow includes not only the wastewater generated from households but also the average infiltration and inflow (I/I) contributions throughout the year. This combined value provides a comprehensive representation of the flow entering the sewer system from each equivalent residential unit.

The average value of 82 gpd/ERU is selected as the basis for determining both present and projected flows and loadings.

Projected wastewater flows and loadings are estimated using historical per capita or ERU data, along with anticipated growth in the service area. These projections are essential for identifying the future needs of the wastewater system and planning for adequate service capacity.

### 5.3.2 Estimating Average Base Sanitary Flow (BSF)  
Water consumption data has been utilized to examine and estimate the average wastewater flow per ERU, excluding infiltration and inflow. Available flow meter data from houses between 2019 to 2023 were analyzed to calculate the base sanitary sewer flow and develop wastewater ERUs.

The calculation is based on residential winter water use, as irrigation during this period is typically negligible, and the majority of water consumed ends up at the wastewater treatment facility (WWTF). Based on experience with similar sized communities, it is estimated that 90 to 100 percent of winter water use contributes to wastewater flows.

For the town of Spangle, it was assumed that 90 percent of winter water use ends up as influent to the treatment facility. The remaining 10 percent accounts for losses due to outdoor and indoor

---

domestic use, and other miscellaneous uses. Using this method, the Average Base Sanitary Flow (BSF) is calculated approximately 105 gallons per day (gpd).

*Note: It was assumed that 90 percent of winter water use ends up as influent to the treatment facility.

The base sanitary flow during winter (105 gpd) is higher than the annual average flow (82 gpd). This variation is expected due to seasonal changes in water usage patterns. While the annual average flow accounts for year-round fluctuations, including infiltration and inflow entering the sewer system, winter months typically see an increase in indoor water use. During colder months, people spend significantly more time indoors, leading to higher water consumption for various household activities. Longer and hotter showers are common as individuals seek warmth and comfort, directly contributing to increased wastewater flow. Additionally, more frequent cooking and dishwashing occur in winter, as families prepare warm meals at home rather than dining out. Laundry demands also rise due to heavier clothing, rain and snow gear, and additional layering, all of which require more frequent washing. Furthermore, in colder climates, some homeowners let faucets drip to prevent pipes from freezing, leading to small but continuous increases in water flow. Certain heating systems and humidifiers also utilize water-based mechanisms, further adding to the overall wastewater volume. Collectively, these factors explain why the base sanitary flow during winter may be slightly higher than the annual average flow, even when excluding infiltration and inflow.

## 5.3.3 Peak Hour Flow

Peak hour flow represents the maximum flow rate expected within a single hour. This value is crucial for designing sewer system components, including pipes, pumps, and treatment facilities, to handle peak loads without overflow or failure. Where flow records are available, analyzing at least two years of data provides an accurate estimate of peak-to-average flow rate factors.

The wastewater treatment plant Discharge Monitoring Report (DMR) from 2018 to 2023 recorded the highest daily flow of 55,652 gallons per day (gpd) on December 27, 2022. This peak flow occurred during the third day of a 72-hour storm event, which significantly impacted the system due to prolonged rainfall and elevated groundwater levels. The rainfall depths recorded for this storm event were:

---

<th>Rainfall Depth (in)</th>

<td>December 25, 2022</td>
<td>0.21</td>
</tr>

<td>December 26, 2022</td>
<td>1.05</td>
</tr>

<td>December 27, 2022</td>
<td>0.98</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

### Peak Hour Flow Calculation
Highest Daily Volume: 55652 gallons or 391.9 gpd /ERU (Total ERU= 142 for the year 2022, refer to Table5.2). The highest daily flow translates to an average daily flow rate of **0.27 gallons per minute (gpm).** To estimate the peak hourly flow, a diurnal multiplier of **1.6** was applied to the highest daily average flow rate:

$$
\text{Peak Hour Flow} = 0.27 \, \text{gpm} \times 1.6 = 0.44 \, \text{gpm}
$$

**Assumptions and Simplifications**  
Since the service area does not have significant contributions from commercial or institutional users, it is assumed that the peak hourly flow occurs simultaneously across all sources. This eliminates the need to calculate peak hourly flow separately for different entities, avoiding potential overestimation of the total peak flow. By accounting for these factors, the peak hourly flow estimation provides a conservative design for the sewer system's critical infrastructure.

#### 5.3.4 Minimum Flow
Average daily minimum flows are used to determine if velocities are adequate to prevent the buildup of solids in the sewer. The recorded minimum daily volume to the treatment plant is 3,146 gpd, which occurred on September 7, 2020.

Minimum Daily Volume: 3146 gallons or 22.96 gpd /ERU (Total ERU= 137 for the year 2020)

The minimum daily flow translates to an average daily flow rate of **0.016 gallons per minute (gpm).**

To estimate the peak hourly flow, a diurnal multiplier of **1.6** was applied to the highest daily average flow rate:

$$
\text{Peak Hour Flow} = 0.016 \, \text{gpm} \times 1.6 = 0.025 \, \text{gpm}
$$

The logic behind using the maximum multiplier for the minimum flow is that it helps estimate the highest flow during the minimum daily flow period, which is essential for ensuring the self-cleansing velocity of the sewer system over a 24-hour period. The self-cleansing velocity is the minimum velocity required to prevent solids from settling in the sewer pipes. By applying the multiplier, we account for potential flow within the day, ensuring that even during periods of low flow, the system can effectively transport solids and prevent blockages. This approach helps

---

design the sewer system to maintain operational efficiency, especially during times of reduced flow.

## 5.3.5 Dry Weather Flow

The total flow in a sewer system during dry weather, referred to as Average Dry Weather Flow (ADWF), occurs over an extended dry period of 7 to 14 days with seasonally high groundwater levels. This flow consists of sanitary flow (**Qₛ**) and groundwater infiltration (**Q_GWI**), excluding significant commercial discharges.

$$
\mathbf{Q_{ADWF} = Q_s * Q_{GWI}}
$$

Where  
**Q_ADWF** = total flow in sewer during dry weather (ft³/s or gpd, m³/s)  
**Qₛ** = flow from sanitary sources (ft³/s or gpd, m³/s)  
**Q_GWI** = groundwater infiltration (ft³/s or gpd, m³/s)  

It assumes no additional inflow during dry weather conditions. Groundwater levels in eastern Washington are typically highest in late winter and spring. To quantify ADWF, Discharge Monitoring Reports (DMRs) were reviewed for the period from January 2018 to July 2024. By examining precipitation data obtained from Rosalia NOAA Daily Precipitation Data for 2018–2024, a dry period was identified from April 16th to April 22nd, lasting seven days, with an observed average flow of 9,527.6 gallons per day (gpd).

As shown in Table 5.4: Total Dry Weather Flow, during the selected dry weather period from April 16 to April 22, 2020, no unusual flow events were observed, such as significant discharges from commercial customers or community events. The data indicates a slight increase in flow over the weekend, which is a common pattern, while weekday flows remained consistent. No abrupt or anomalous data points were identified, ensuring that the entire dataset was suitable for use in calculating the **Average Dry Weather Flow (ADWF)** during this period.

From Table 2.3, the town had a population of 273 people. The average dry weather flow per capita was calculated at **34.9 gallons per day per capita (gpdc)**, which is significantly lower than the

---

typical benchmark of **120 gpdc**. This indicates a relatively low per-person water usage and groundwater infiltration into the system and as a result efficient system performance under dry weather conditions. The average dry weather flow per ERU is calculated based on the average number of people per household, as shown in Chart 2.2, which is 79.6 gpd.

Based on the analysis, the observed per capita flow of 34.9 gpdc is significantly below the EPA's 120 gpdc guideline, demonstrating that the town's sewer system operates efficiently with minimal impact from groundwater infiltration. This low per capita flow indicates compliance with EPA standards, eliminating the need for additional infiltration analysis or costly treatment upgrades. The findings underscore the adequacy of the existing infrastructure and its ability to manage dry weather flows effectively, ensuring cost-efficient operation and adherence to regulatory requirements.

The flow rate of 120 gallons per capita per day (gpcd) for infiltration analysis consists of two components: 70 gpcd for domestic wastewater base flow and 50 gpcd for non-excessive infiltration. This is a national average based on a survey of approximately 270 standard metropolitan statistical area cities. As noted, the average base flow used to establish this criterion is 70 gpcd, which is significantly higher than the average dry weather per capita flow (34.9 gpcd). This indicates that, in the town of Spangle, the average sewage generated per household is much lower than the national average.

### 5.3.6 Wet Weather Flow

During and after rainfall, an additional flow, known as **rainfall-derived infiltration and inflow (RDII)**, enters the wastewater collection system. RDII is the portion of the total flow that rises above the usual dry weather flow pattern. This expands the total flow equation to account for RDII as follows:

$$
Q_{WWF} = Q_s + Q_{GWI} + Q_{RDII}
$$

Or:

$$
Q_{WWF} = Q_{ADWF} + Q_{RDII}
$$

Where:  
- \( Q_{WWF} \) = total flow in the sewer (measured in ft³/s, gpd, or m³/s)  
- \( Q_s \) = flow from sanitary sources (ft³/s, gpd, or m³/s)  
- \( Q_{GWI} \) = groundwater infiltration (ft³/s, gpd, or m³/s)  
- \( Q_{RDII} \) = rainfall-derived infiltration and inflow (ft³/s, gpd, or m³/s)  

This equation reflects how rainfall events contribute an additional flow component to the sewer system, which is important for analyzing and managing system capacity and performance.

Using the precipitation data provided by NOAA, The analysis of the highest back-to-back precipitation was performed using daily precipitation data from the following areas and periods:

---

* **Spangle, Washington:** June 2021 to July 2024  
* **Rosalia, Washington:** 2018 to 2024  
* **Paradise, Washington:** July 2018 to July 2024  

The wet weather flow is the highest daily flow during and immediately after a significant storm event, including sanitary flow, infiltration, and inflow. To quantify the wet weather flow, the same approach as described in the dry weather section was used, but the DMR data was examined during the period in which the maximum flow into the treatment plant was observed. This maximum flow coincided with the highest recorded precipitation event in the region, highlighting the impact of wet weather on treatment plant operations.

The data indicated that the highest cumulative precipitation occurred between December 26, 2022, and December 27, 2022, with a total of 2.03 inches (1.05 inches on December 26 and 0.98 inches on December 27) in the region. This highest precipitation coincides with the highest recorded flow at the treatment plant, which occurred on December 27, 2022, with a flow of 55,652 gallons per day (GPD). This is the highest recorded flow from the DMR dataset, which covers the period from January 2018 to July 2024.

From Table 2.3, the town had a population of 280 people. The average wet weather flow per capita was calculated at 198.75 gallons per day per capita (gpdc), which is significantly lower than the benchmark of 275 gpdc specified by *EPA, Infiltration/Inflow: I/I Analysis and Project Certification*, 97-03, May 1985. Since the wet weather flow per capita does not exceed 275 gpcd, the amount of inflow is considered non excessive. Therefore, further study of inflow and correction alternatives are not required. However, if the treatment plant experiences hydraulic overloads during storm events, further study will be required regardless of the wet weather flow, even when the flow is below the 275 gpcd threshold.

## 5.4 ERU Estimates and Projections

The total commercial and public Equivalent Residential Units (ERUs) were calculated using existing water meter data. The total winter water consumption for each commercial and public entity was determined, reduced by 10 percent (as described in Section 2: BSF Estimation), and then divided by the average daily base sanitary flow of 105 gpd to determine the number of ERUs.

As discussed in *Chapter 2: Population Estimates and Projections*, two scenarios were considered for the town’s future population growth and rate of increase. The first scenario assumes continued organic population growth without the Wheatland Estates housing development, while the second scenario incorporates the impact of the planned housing expansion. These scenarios serve as the basis for estimating future total ERUs.

### Scenario 1 – Organic Growth (Without Wheatland Estates or Other Major Developments)

To estimate the number of housing units under this scenario, Table 2.6 was used. The total number of Equivalent Residential Units (ERUs) was estimated by averaging the projected populations derived from Method 1 – Population Trend Analysis and Method 2 – Housing Unit-Based Projection and dividing the result by the average household size of 2.28.

---

The commercial/public ERU growth was estimated using the same growth rate as residential ERUs. The total ERUs were then calculated as the sum of the total residential ERUs and commercial/public ERUs. The results are summarized in the Table 5.4.

The same calculation method was applied to the second scenario, where the planned Wheatland Estates housing development is considered. The population estimate derived from Method 2 – Housing Unit-Based Projection (Wheatland Estates) in Table 2.6 was used to estimate the total ERUs.

## 5.6 Peaking Factors

Based on the provided flow data, flow factors were calculated by comparing each flow parameter to the Average Daily Flow (ADF) to determine variations in system demand. The Peaking Factors were derived by taking the ratio of peak flows, such as Peak Hour Design Flow (PHDF) and Peak Wet Weather Flow (PWWF), to ADF, highlighting the expected surges in wastewater generation. The Maximum Month Flow (MMF) Factor represents the seasonal variations, while the Maximum Dry Weather Flow (MDWF) Factor accounts for daily dry weather peak conditions. Additionally, the Minimum Flow Factor indicates the lowest expected flow relative to ADF. These factors were averaged over the period from 2025 to 2045 to provide a representative assessment of system performance. The summarized flow ratios are presented in the table below:

<table>
<thead>
<tr>
<th colspan="2">TABLE EXISTING AND PROJECTED WASTEWATER CHARACTERISTICS.21- PEAKING FACTORS</th>
</tr>
</thead>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td><b>Flow Factor</b></td>
<td><b>Average Ratio<br>(2025–2045)</b></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Peak Wet Weather Flow Factor (PWWF/ADF)</td>
<td>6.91</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Maximum Month Flow Factor (MMF/ADF)</td>
<td>2.44</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Maximum Dry Weather Flow Factor (MDWF/ADF)</td>
<td>1.55</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Peak Hourly Peaking Factor (PHDF/ADF)</td>
<td>7.73</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Peak Instantaneous Design Flow (PIDF)*</td>
<td>8.50</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Minimum Flow Factor (Min Flow/ADF)</td>
<td>0.30</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

*It was assumed that the instantaneous peak design flow is 10% higher than the peak hourly design flow.

## 5.7 Wastewater Influent Characterization

Spangle's discharge monitoring reports provide data on influent pollutant concentrations, including BOD₅, TSS, and NH₃ loadings. However, the location where samples are collected may impact the accuracy of the data and the representation of influent characteristics. This is because the facility’s scum removal device on the secondary clarifier recycles cleaner water back to the

---

headworks, and influent samples are taken after this recycled water is introduced. Consequently, the samples may not fully reflect the true conditions of the influent wastewater.

Loadings for BOD₅, TSS, and NH₃ from January 2018 through December 2024 are shown in Charts 5.1 through 5.3, with annual average, maximum month, and peak day loadings summarized in Table 5.9.

The charts illustrate the relationship between Average Monthly Precipitation (in blue, plotted with a reversed secondary vertical axis), Average Monthly BOD₅ (Biochemical Oxygen Demand, in green), and TSS (Total Suspended Solids, in dark red) over time, spanning from January 2018 to July 2024.

Precipitation levels vary significantly across the years. Peaks are observed in months such as June 2020 (3.755 inches) and December 2022 (3.77 inches). However, several months exhibit low precipitation, such as August 2020 (0.035 inches) and July 2021 (0.06 inches).

---

BOD₅ levels show significant variability, with distinct peaks during months of low precipitation, possibly due to reduced dilution. TSS values also exhibit notable fluctuations, with some spikes potentially correlating with specific rainfall patterns or runoff conditions.

During months with higher precipitation, BOD₅ and TSS levels tend to decrease, likely due to the dilution effects from increased water volume entering the sewer system.

In months with lower precipitation, BOD₅ levels tend to increase, possibly due to reduced dilution and higher concentrations of organic material in the wastewater.

Overall, there is no significant correlation between precipitation and BOD₅ or TSS levels. The calculated correlation coefficients are 0.02 and 0.03, respectively, indicating no meaningful relationship between these parameters.

The chart shows average monthly precipitation and ammonium (NH₄⁺) concentrations over time, revealing a fluctuating relationship between the two variables. While ammonium levels sometimes correspond to precipitation, the pattern is not linear. For example, in months with high precipitation like January 2020 (3.635 inches), ammonium levels were low (0.1 mg/L), while in months with lower precipitation, such as February 2023 (0.74 inches), ammonium levels were similarly low (0.125 mg/L).

However, certain months with high precipitation, such as June 2021 (0.62 inches), exhibit significant peaks in ammonium levels (8.05 mg/L), suggesting that factors beyond precipitation, like soil leaching or environmental conditions, may influence ammonium concentrations. Additionally, ammonium levels tend to rise during the agricultural season (late May to early October), as seen in June 2021, October 2021, and June 2022. This trend indicates that agricultural activities, including fertilization and runoff, contribute to increased ammonium levels, regardless of precipitation amounts.

## 5.8 Projected Wastewater Loads

Tables 5.13 through #.# present the projected wastewater loads for the planning period. These projections will serve as the foundation for evaluating system capacity, planning infrastructure upgrades, optimizing design and operations, and guiding maintenance, financial planning, funding strategies, and budgeting.

This table has been developed using the average wastewater loads calculated and presented in Table 2—Wastewater Influent Characterization for Average Daily Flow (AADF), Average Dry Weather Flow (ADWF), Maximum Dry Weather Flow (MDWF), and Minimum Flow.

For the Base Sanitary Flow, the average influent BOD₅ and TSS during January through May and October through December were used (see table #.#). The Base Sanitary Flow was calculated using water meter data from these months, as the influent averages during this period are more representative compared to the annual average daily flow.

For Average Wet Weather Flow (AWWF) and Maximum Week Flow, the recorded loads corresponding to the periods when these flows occurred were used for calculation as shown in table #.#

        <td>148.7</td>
        <td>4.49</td>

# Chapter 6: Collection System Improvements

## 6.1 Introduction  
The purpose of this chapter is to assess and evaluate the existing collection system based on the initial and design years of the planning period and to identify necessary improvements. This assessment will determine upgrades required to accommodate population growth and evaluate the impact of new development on the existing sewer infrastructure. Additionally, it will identify new sewer infrastructure that will be integrated into the system and analyze the effects of these developments on system capacity and performance. Based on these findings, a pre-planning strategy and recommended upgrades will be proposed to ensure the collection system remains efficient and capable of meeting future demands.

## 6.2 Existing Sewer Infrastructure  
The existing sewer collection system in Spangle is divided into four sewersheds, as shown in Figure 6.1. Each area is primarily served by 6 or 8-inch sewer laterals and an 8 inch collector, except for Sewershed-3, which covers a small area and is served by a single lateral. All these collectors and the lateral are connected eventually to the lift station and collected sewage from entire town is pumped by that only lift station to wastewater treatment plant located at northeast corner of city limit. As discussed in Chapter 4 and illustrated in Figure #.#, the majority of the sewer pipes in the system are 8 inches in diameter. To evaluate the system’s adequacy, a sewer capacity analysis was conducted. The results were compared to the projected flow requirements outlined in Chapter 5, Projected Flows, to ensure that the system meets current and future capacity needs. For more detailed information on the existing sewer system, please refer to Chapter 4: Existing Sewer System.

## 6.3 Future Sewer Infrastructure  
In Chapter 2 - Planning Area Characteristics, future population growth was estimated based on two scenarios:

* Scenario One: Organic Growth (Without Wheatland Estates Development)  
* Scenario Two: Growth with Planned Housing expansion (Wheatland Estates Development)  

Future sewer infrastructure expansion and development are aligned and investigated under these two scenarios. The shape and form of development within existing urban growth areas remain uncertain, as they depend on vacant property owners within the current service area. To evaluate the impact on the sewer system, a build-out scenario was assumed, where vacant lots within the service area would be developed for housing. Currently, there are approximately 13 vacant lots available within the sewer service area, which would generate additional wastewater flows at various locations in town. The existing sewer system’s capacity under this built-out scenario was evaluated as the first scenario:

**Ultimate Build-Out Within Existing Sewer Service Area**

---

This scenario assumes full development of all vacant lots currently within the sewer service area, generating additional wastewater loads. The capacity of the existing sewer infrastructure, including the collection system, lift station, and force main, was analyzed to determine if it can accommodate these projected flows.

Beyond this ultimate built-out scenario, additional growth will add further demand to the sewer system, requiring new infrastructure. Future growth outside this built-out phase is analyzed under two additional capacity scenarios:

**FIGURE COLLECTION SYSTEM IMPROVEMENTS.14: SEWERSHEDS MAP**

[Place holder]

This Photo by Unknown Author is licensed under CC BY-SA

---

**Sporadic Development Under Scenario One (Organic Growth)**

This scenario considers incremental residential development occurring within the town limits over time. As properties are developed individually, the sewer system infrastructure will expand accordingly. However, the existing sewer collection and conveyance system may experience localized capacity constraints.

As discussed above, there are four collector sewers connected to the lift station. Adding an additional collector may not be feasible and could require structural modifications to the existing pipes near the lift station to accommodate an additional pipeline. Further evaluation is needed to determine if a junction chamber or sewer manifold is required. Under this scenario, the capacity of the lift station and force main has been evaluated to assess whether modifications are necessary. Under this scenario, the capacity of the lift station and force main was evaluated to determine whether modifications are necessary.

**Major Housing Development Under Scenario Two (Wheatland Estates Development)**

This scenario evaluates the impact of a planned, large-scale residential development. Wheatland Estates is expected to introduce a significant number of new homes, leading to a substantial increase in wastewater generation. The analysis focuses on whether the existing lift station, force main, can accommodate this surge in demand, or if major system upgrades—including increased lift station capacity and additional sewer mains—are required.

Since the entire town’s sewer collection system is directed to the lift station, which is located at the lowest point relative to the surrounding area (as detailed in Chapter 2, Section: Topography of the Town), the capacity of the lift station was evaluated under each of these three scenarios. The analysis determines whether the lift station can handle projected flows or if additional infrastructure investments are needed to support future growth.

### 6.4 Collection System Analysis

A computer-based hydraulic model of the existing sewer system in Spangle was developed using Autodesk Storm and Sanitary Analysis (SSA) software. The model includes the entire sewer collection system, incorporating gravity mains, force mains, and lift station. It was created using

---

the best available data, including GIS records, as-built drawings, survey data, and information provided by the Town of Spangle. The output from this analysis was used to assess the capacity of the existing collection system and identify necessary improvements to accommodate future wastewater flows. This model can be updated and maintained as a valuable planning tool for future infrastructure development.

Autodesk Storm and Sanitary Analysis (SSA) is a comprehensive modeling software that integrates stormwater and sanitary sewer analysis into a single platform. This integration allows for the simultaneous evaluation of stormwater inflow and infiltration (I&I) within the sanitary sewer system, providing a more accurate representation of real-world conditions. SSA helps assess the effects of stormwater entering the sewer network, analyze system capacity under wet weather conditions, and determine the impact of I&I on wastewater infrastructure. By modeling both systems together, SSA supports informed decision-making for infrastructure planning and ensures efficient and resilient sewer system management.

A key feature of SSA is its ability to evaluate each pipe's capacity alongside existing flow conditions. This functionality allows for a detailed assessment of current infrastructure, enabling timely decisions on necessary improvements. By calculating the design flow capacity of pipes under gravity flow conditions at 100% full, SSA provides a direct comparison between existing flow and maximum pipe capacity. This comparison is essential for identifying undersized pipes and determining where upgrades are needed to meet both present and future demands.

SSA also applies hydraulic equations to simulate flow behavior and evaluate surcharging conditions under various loading scenarios. Additionally, it integrates with Civil 3D, streamlining design workflows and enhancing data management for sewer system planning. This combination of capabilities makes SSA a valuable tool for analyzing and managing stormwater and sanitary sewer infrastructure.

### 6.4.1 Model Assumptions and Capacity Evaluation

According to the Orange Book (The Criteria for Sewage Works Design by the Washington State Department of Ecology), the recommended design capacity for gravity sewer pipes is 0.7 times the pipe diameter (0.7d) under peak flow conditions. However, Autodesk Storm and Sanitary Analysis (SSA) calculates pipe capacity based on a full-flow condition (100% full diameter). To align with the Orange Book standard, an equivalent diameter corresponding to 0.7d flow conditions was determined and applied in the model to accurately evaluate sewer capacity under peak flow scenarios.

To adjust for 0.7d flow conditions, the equivalent diameters were calculated based on standard sewer pipe sizes:

* 8-inch pipe: Equivalent diameter for 0.7d flow ≈ 7.2 inches
* 6-inch pipe: Equivalent diameter for 0.7d flow ≈ 5.4 inches

For hydraulic analysis, the Manning's roughness coefficient (n) is a key parameter used to determine flow resistance within the sewer pipes. The Orange Book recommends using n = 0.013

---

for the design of new gravity sewer pipes and n = 0.015 for evaluating existing sewer systems to account for aging, sediment buildup, and potential surface roughness. In this model, a Manning’s n value of 0.015 was applied to reflect real-world conditions in the assessment of the existing sewer network.

## 6.4.2 Hydraulic Analysis Results
The analysis results are discussed in two parts. The first part focuses on the gravity collection system, which evaluates the capacity of the existing sewer collection system. The second part covers the analysis of the lift station and force main.

## 6.4.3 P Collection system

**Existing Condition**  
The analysis of the existing sewer collection system was conducted for both the Peak Hourly Design Flow (PHDF) and the Peak Instantaneous Design Flow (PIDF) to assess the system’s capacity under these critical flow conditions. The results of the analysis are presented in Tables ## and ## appendix ##, which summarize the findings for each flow condition.

For the Peak Hourly Design Flow (PHDF), the existing infrastructure, including gravity mains, force mains, and the lift station, was evaluated for its ability to handle the anticipated flow. Similarly, the Peak Instantaneous Design Flow (PIDF), which represents the highest flow expected in a very short duration, was also analyzed. Both flow conditions were modeled under the current system configuration to assess the adequacy of the sewer infrastructure in handling these extreme flow scenarios.

As detailed in the analysis results, the system capacity is found to be adequate for both the Peak Hourly Design Flow and the Peak Instantaneous Design Flow under existing conditions. The existing sewer pipes, lift station, and force mains are capable of managing the projected flows without any risk of overloading. This indicates that the current system can handle the anticipated wastewater flows during peak conditions, ensuring reliable operation for the town's sewer infrastructure.

**Ultimate Build-Out Within Existing Sewer Service Area**  
As shown by the capacity analysis of the existing condition, the collection system capacity is well beyond the current flow entering the system. The addition of 13 residential units or commercial buildings within the existing sewer service area will not significantly impact the system. The infrastructure can continue to function effectively without reaching its maximum capacity, ensuring that future developments within the service area will not overwhelm the existing system. This indicates that the current sewer collection system is sufficiently sized to handle both present and future demands from local development.

**Other Housing Development Scenarios**  
For other housing development scenarios, such as sporadic housing development outside the current developed areas or major housing development like the Wheatland Estates housing

---

development, new sewer collection systems will need to be designed and constructed to accommodate the corresponding development. Since these developments fall outside the existing service area, they will not impact the current sewer collection system. Therefore, no further investigation into the existing infrastructure capacity is required under these scenarios, as the development will necessitate the construction of new infrastructure to meet the increased demand.

## 6.4.4 Lift Station and Force Main

The Spangle Lift Station is equipped with a wet well and two 4B2D Vacuum No-Clog Pumps, each operating at 1,760 RPM with 8 3/4" impellers mounted on top of the wet well. These pumps are designed to handle both high-flow and solids-laden liquids, utilizing vacuum technology to ensure smooth and continuous operation while preventing clogging and reducing maintenance requirements. Currently, the pumps efficiently handle wastewater collected from the entire town without any operational issues.

The wet well has a low liquid level of 2,400 feet, and the pumps transfer sewage from this point to the treatment plant headworks. The force main is a 2,200-foot, 4-inch pipeline that conveys the wastewater to the treatment plant. The highest water level at the headworks is 2,438 feet. To analyze the pump capacity and evaluate the overall lift station performance, a system curve and pump performance curve have been developed (see Appendix #.#). Given the town’s topography within the city limits and the fact that the existing lift station is at the lowest point, it was assumed that both the existing and future sewer collection systems would be directed to this lift station. Therefore, the lift station was analyzed under both existing conditions and future development scenarios to determine whether it is sufficient under each scenario or if an upgrade or a new lift station will be required.

**Organic Growth (Without Wheatland Estates or Other Major Developments)**  
The Spangle Lift Station has a pumping capacity of 190 GPM at a total dynamic head of 78.16 feet. Under the organic growth scenario, which excludes major developments such as Wheatland Estates, the peak instantaneous design flow (PIDF) is projected to reach 178.83 GPM by 2045, remaining within the station’s pumping capacity. Similarly, the peak hour design flow (PHDF) and peak wet weather flow (PWWF) also stay below the station’s maximum capacity throughout the planning period, confirming that the lift station is adequate for handling wastewater demand under organic growth conditions through 2045. However, any additional developments beyond organic growth could exceed the station’s capacity, requiring further evaluation to determine whether system upgrades or a new lift station will be necessary.

In force main, maintaining an optimal flow velocity is critical to ensure efficient operation and minimize maintenance needs. According to industry guidelines, a minimum self-scouring velocity of 2 feet per second (fps) should be maintained under pumping conditions to prevent the settling of solids in the pipe unless flushing facilities are provided. On the other hand, the velocity should not exceed 8 fps to avoid issues like pipe wear, water hammer, and excessive energy consumption. For reducing maintenance costs and preventing the accumulation of solids, the optimum velocity typically falls between 3.5 and 5 fps. At the maximum optimum velocity of 5 fps, the corresponding flow rate for the existing 4-inch force main is 196 gallons per minute (gpm), which is adequate for organic growth throughout the design period. However, under the growth scenario with the Wheatland Estates development, a force main upgrade will be required as the new housing development begins, as the projected flows exceed the system's capacity.

# Chapter 7: Wastewater Planning Area Characteristic

## 7.1 Land Application of Wastewater Effluent

Land application of wastewater is a sustainable method for managing and treating wastewater. It involves applying treated or partially treated wastewater to the land, where natural processes help remove contaminants. This method supports groundwater recharge, enhances soil health, and promotes agricultural or forestry growth by providing essential nutrients.

Land application and treatment can be categorized into three main types:

1. Slow Rate (Irrigation)  
2. Overland Flow  
3. Infiltration-Percolation  

As mentioned in Chapter 2, the most suitable soil for land application is **Naff Silt Loam (Type 6061)**, found in the areas surrounding the treatment plant. This soil is well-suited for Slow Rate Land Application due to its specific characteristics, but it is less compatible with the other two methods.

In the Slow Rate (SR) method of wastewater treatment, the expected quality of the effluent after pre-treatment is typically very high. The treated wastewater is applied to land at a controlled rate, where natural processes such as percolation, evaporation, and plant uptake help further remove contaminants. As a result, the effluent quality is expected to meet strict criteria, with low concentrations of pollutants. Typical values for effluent quality include biochemical oxygen demand (BOD₅) of less than 2 mg/L, total suspended solids (TSS) less than 1 mg/L, ammonia nitrogen (NH₃/NH₄) below 0.5 mg/L, total nitrogen (N) around 3 mg/L, total phosphorus (P) less than 0.1 mg/L, and fecal coliforms at levels lower than 1 colony forming unit per 100 mL. (These values are from Table 1-3, EPA Process Design Manual: Land Treatment of Municipal Wastewater Effluents).

### Soil Properties and Site Characteristics

The required site characteristics for the Slow Rate (SR) treatment method and the characteristics of the 6061—Naff Silt Loam (0 to 8% Slopes) soil type are listed in the table below.

**FIGURE WASTEWATER PLANNING AREA CHARACTERISTIC.15: SUITABILITY OF 6061—NAFF SILT LOAM FOR SLOW RATE WASTEWATER LAND APPLICATION**

As shown in the table, the 6061—Naff Silt Loam soil meets all the required characteristics for the Slow Rate treatment method. It satisfies the slope, soil permeability, and groundwater depth requirements, making it suitable for land application under the SR treatment method.

Slow Rate (SR) Land Treatment involves applying wastewater to a vegetated soil surface where it is treated as it moves through the plant root/soil matrix. Key processes like infiltration, percolation, and evapotranspiration help remove solids and treat the water biologically, chemically, and physically. The system prevents off-site runoff, ensuring all treated water stays within the site. SR land treatment can serve multiple purposes, including wastewater treatment, crop production, water conservation, and green space development.

## 7.2 Wastewater Constituents and Removal Mechanisms in Slow-Rate (SR) Systems

**Biochemical Oxygen Demand (BOD)**  
The SR system reduces BOD through processes like filtration, absorption, and biological oxidation in the soil's upper layers. For the system to work well, periodic application of wastewater is needed to keep aerobic conditions in the soil. BOD removal efficiency is high as long as these conditions are maintained, with typical BOD loading rates ranging from 20 to 200 kg BOD/acre-d.

**Total Suspended Solids (TSS)**  
TSS are removed by filtration in the SR system, as wastewater passes through the soil. This ensures high-quality effluent by trapping particles in the soil.

**pH**  
The ideal pH for biological treatment is between 5 and 9. Soil has a good buffer capacity, which helps maintain a stable pH. If the pH drops below 6, it can cause metals to leach into groundwater. A pH of 6 or higher is preferred to avoid this risk. Crops can tolerate a wide range of pH levels, but the best range is between 6.4 and 8.4.

**Effectiveness in Pathogen Removal**  
The SR process is very effective in removing pathogens, particularly fecal coliforms, from wastewater. It typically achieves a significant reduction in pathogens (about five logs) within the top 2 feet of soil. This happens through natural soil processes like adsorption, desiccation, predation, and UV radiation.

**Groundwater Contamination**  
The risk of groundwater contamination from pathogens in SR systems is low, especially in soils like silt loam and silty clay loam. These soils effectively remove pathogens through filtration and adsorption, ensuring minimal contamination by the time wastewater reaches groundwater.

---

**Crop Contamination**  
In SR systems using treated and disinfected effluent, the risk of crop contamination is minimal. Proper treatment and disinfection reduce the chance of pathogens on crop surfaces or inside the plants' roots. Most viruses die within a few days on crops, further minimizing risks.

**Aerosol Management**  
When wastewater is applied using low-pressure sprinklers, small airborne particles (aerosols) can form. However, these particles are usually low in concentration and are quickly reduced by UV light, heat, and desiccation, making them no more of a health risk than other common environments.

**Metal Accumulation and Management**  
SR systems need to be monitored for metal accumulation in the soil, as metals like cadmium, lead, and mercury can enter the food chain. However, proper management can minimize this risk. The concentration of recently applied wastewater has a greater impact on metal uptake by crops than the total accumulation in the soil. Different crops take up metals at different rates, leafy vegetables (e.g., Swiss chard) tend to accumulate more metals compared to other crops. Monitoring crop type is important if metal uptake is a concern.

Metals like zinc and copper are less toxic, but cadmium remains a concern. Grazing animals can also accumulate metals, but under controlled conditions, the risks are low.

**Nitrogen Removal**  
Nitrogen removal in SR systems is complex. It involves different nitrogen forms, such as ammonia, nitrate, and organic nitrogen. Nitrification can convert ammonia to nitrate, but nitrogen removal depends on soil conditions and microbial activity. Excess nitrogen can cause nitrate leaching, which is a concern near drinking water sources.

The nitrogen form most suitable for land application in SR systems is ammonia (or ammonium) and organic nitrogen, as these less oxidized forms allow the soil microbes to effectively process and remove nitrogen through natural biological transformations. When nitrogen enters the system mainly as nitrate, it is more difficult to remove because nitrate is highly mobile in soil and requires special anaerobic conditions for removal, increasing the risk of groundwater contamination. Therefore, wastewater with higher concentrations of ammonia and organic nitrogen is preferred for effective nitrogen removal in land treatment systems.

Experience with all three land treatment processes demonstrates that the less oxidized the nitrogen is when entering the land treatment system, the more effective the retention and overall nitrogen removal will be.

Under good conditions (enough alkalinity, proper temperature), 5–50 mg/L of ammonium per day can be nitrified. In fine-textured soil, considering only the top four inches where ammonium is adsorbed, this can result in up to 67 kg/ha (60 lb/acre) of ammonium converted to nitrate per year.

**SR Method and Precautions**  
In Spray Irrigation systems, careful management is required to prevent nitrogen from exceeding safe limits. The U.S. primary drinking water standard for nitrate (as N) is 10 mg/L. The main concern in SR is the conversion of wastewater nitrogen to nitrate and its potential percolation to drinking water aquifers. When potable aquifers, sole source aquifers, or wellhead protection

---

areas are involved, current guidance requires that all drinking water standards be met at the land treatment project boundary.

As a result, nitrogen often becomes the limiting design parameter (LDP) for SR systems because of its relatively high concentration compared to other drinking water parameters. Appropriate measures—such as monitoring application rates, controlling irrigation timing, and ensuring sufficient soil treatment—must be taken to avoid discharging nitrogen beyond acceptable limits. This ensures that nitrogen does not exceed the allowable concentrations and protects groundwater quality.

**Phosphorus Removal**  
Phosphorus is removed through plant uptake, biological processes, and chemical reactions in the soil. The primary mechanisms in SR systems are adsorption and precipitation, especially in soils with high clay content. Over time, phosphorus removal may slow, but it doesn't deplete the soil. Proper system design is necessary to manage phosphorus and prevent contamination of nearby water sources.

In SR (slow-rate) land treatment systems, soils with moderate to high clay content, such as Naff silt loam, are well-suited for phosphorus retention. Naff silt loam contains fine-textured horizons with significant clay and iron/aluminum oxide content, which enhance phosphorus adsorption and precipitation. This combination allows the soil to act as a long-term filter, keeping percolate phosphorus concentrations close to natural background levels for extended periods. A baseline soil test is recommended before designing the system to track phosphorus accumulation and determine when levels approach environmental thresholds. As a rule of thumb, approximately 1 foot of soil may become saturated with phosphorus in ~10 years under typical SR loading rates.

### 7.3 Site Planning and Selection  
#### 7.3.1 Initial Site Selection for Land Treatment Application  
In the process of identifying potential sites for land treatment application within the city limits and surrounding areas, a comprehensive evaluation using USDA Soil Web Survey was conducted. Based on the findings, four soil types were identified as suitable for land treatment. These soils were ranked according to their suitability for agricultural wastewater disposal, as listed in Table 1 - Soil Types and Suitability for Agricultural Disposal of Wastewater by Irrigation and Overland Flow in Chapter 2.

The Naff Silt Loam (Type 6061) was identified as the most suitable soil type. This soil type is abundant in the region around the wastewater treatment plant, which is advantageous due to its proximity to the wastewater source, thereby reducing transmission costs.

**Key Characteristics of the Naff Silt Loam:**  
* Soil Texture: Silty loam with fine texture, ideal for slow-rate application of wastewater for irrigation purposes.  
* Depth to Groundwater: The soil has a depth of more than 80 inches to the groundwater, ensuring sufficient filtration and preventing contamination of water sources.

---

* Area Coverage: This soil type covers the largest area, making it an ideal candidate for large-scale land treatment applications.

## 7.3.2 Wastewater Characteristics

Wastewater characteristics are essential factors in the design and operation of land treatment systems. These characteristics include average annual flows and concentrations of key constituents such as Biochemical Oxygen Demand (BOD₅), suspended solids, nitrogen, phosphorus, and trace elements.

In the case of the Spangle land application system, the effluent used for irrigation is secondary treated and disinfected with chlorination. As a result, the design is not limited by BOD₅ or suspended solids (TSS) loading. Instead, nitrogen loading will likely be the controlling factor for design. The nitrogen removal capacity will depend on crop uptake and system management practices. Consequently, the required land area for application and treatment will be determined primarily based on nitrogen loading considerations. While phosphorus or trace elements may occasionally impact the design, this is generally uncommon, and nitrogen remains the primary factor influencing system capacity.

As mentioned, the preapplication treatment has little effect on the loading rate for SR systems. For preliminary estimates, a loading rate of 1.5 inches per week for agricultural applications and 1 inch per week for forestry applications is selected. These rates are considered very conservative. Once the potential site has been analyzed and its ability to meet discharge requirements is assessed, the loading rates can be modified accordingly.

In Spangle, where cold weather and occasional excessive precipitation are common, wastewater storage is essential for flow equalization, system backup, reliability, and effective system management. For SR systems in Spangle, a minimum of 120 days of storage is estimated based on climatic factors, as shown in EPA’s Process Design Manual, Figure 5-2. This estimate accounts for cold weather, excessive precipitation, and crop management. However, additional storage days will be needed to cover the time required for harvesting and planting agricultural crops. While Figure 5-2 provides conservative preliminary estimates for storage, these values will be refined through further site-specific evaluations to more accurately determine the required storage needs.

In Eastern Washington, the irrigation season generally begins in late March or early April. According to the East Columbia Basin Irrigation District, canal filling starts on March 20th. This marks the start of the irrigation period, which typically continues through late September or early October, lasting approximately 28 weeks. During this time, wastewater application for land treatment systems can occur, providing a substantial window for managing effluent through irrigation.

A summary of the average annual daily flow (AADF) during the planning period for two town growth scenarios is presented in the tables below. The land area required for the slow rate (SR) application method has been calculated using Formula 5-1, as described in the EPA’s Process Design Manual: Land Treatment of Municipal Wastewater Effluents (September 2006). This

---

calculation considers the Average Daily Wastewater Flows (AADF), a loading rate of 1.5 inches per week, and a 23-week period of application.

## Irrigation System Loading Rates and Water Balance

For irrigation systems, two key loading rates must be evaluated to determine the limiting factor:

Water balance and constituent balance (e.g., nitrogen, phosphorus, organic matter). Assessing both ensures that the system operates efficiently and meets regulatory and environmental constraints.

### Water Balance Components

The water balance consists of inflows and outflows, which must be considered to size the irrigation system properly.

Inflows:

* Wastewater applied
* Precipitation

Outflows:

* Evapotranspiration
* Percolation

For this analysis, the Eastern Washington Stormwater Manual was used to calculate precipitation and evaporation into and out of the effluent storage pond. A spreadsheet #.# was developed

---

(shown on Page # of this report) to properly size the storage capacity. The irrigation period is assumed to be 23 weeks per year. Percolation from the storage basin is assumed to be zero since it will be an earthen-lined basin, preventing seepage losses.

## 7.4 Effluent Storage Pond Design

The effluent storage pond is being planned to collect and manage treated effluent from the Spangle treatment plant for irrigation purposes. To determine the appropriate size for the pond, a similar principle and methodology was applied.

### Design Methodology

The planning of the effluent storage pond is based on a detailed water budget analysis, incorporating monthly inflow data from October 2018 to September 2021, sourced from the Spangle treatment plant. The primary factors influencing the storage capacity include:

1. Inflows: These will consist of treated effluent discharge from the Spangle treatment plant, recorded in cubic feet per month, and monthly precipitation directly into the pond.  
2. Outflows: Water loss through evaporation from the pond surface and irrigation application.  
3. Net Storage Volume: This will represent the difference between inflows and outflows for each month, indicating the amount of water that remains in the pond.

The cumulative water budget was calculated to ensure that the pond design can accommodate the expected variations in effluent and rainfall.

### Freeboard Requirement

A minimum of 1-foot freeboard will be maintained to account for fluctuations in effluent inflow and ensure that overflow is prevented, even during peak inflow periods.

### Climatological Data

Average monthly precipitation and evaporation rates will be sourced from NOAA and the Western Regional Climate Center, ensuring accurate estimates of evaporation losses and monthly precipitation for the design period.

### Infiltration and Percolation Control

The pond will be planned with a geosynthetic liner to prevent groundwater seepage and percolation, ensuring that water loss due to infiltration is minimized and the stored effluent remains within the desired storage capacity.

### Nitrogen Loading Rate and Biolac® Treatment System

The limiting design factor for land application in this study is nitrogen. The Biolac® system treats wastewater by utilizing extended aeration and activated sludge processes to reduce organic matter and convert nitrogen primarily through nitrification and denitrification. However, residual total nitrogen (TN) remains in the effluent, requiring consideration when applied to land.

### Nitrogen Loading Rate Calculation

---

# Given Data:

* Application Rate = 1.5 inches per week  
* Duration = 23 weeks per year  
* Effluent Total Nitrogen (TN) ≈ 15 mg/L (assumed based on typical Biolac® effluent)  

## Conversion Factors:

* 1 inch of water per acre = 27,154 gallons  
* 1 mg/L = 8.34 lb per million gallons (MG)  

## Total Effluent Applied per Acre per Year

* total application depth = 1.5 inches/week × 23 weeks = 34.5 inches/year  

Convert inches to million gallons per acre per year (MG/acre/yr):  
* total effluent applied = (34.5 inches × 27,154 gallons per inch) ÷ 1,000,000 = 0.94 MG/acre/year  

## Nitrogen Loading

* total nitrogen applied = Effluent TN × 8.34 × MG applied  
* total nitrogen applied = 15 mg/L × 8.34 × 0.94 MG/acre/year  
* total nitrogen applied = 117.2 lb/acre/year  

## Crop Nitrogen Uptake Assumptions

Typical crop nitrogen uptake values are considered:  
* Corn: 150 lb/acre/yr  
* Reed canary grass: 230–400 lb/acre/yr  

For this study, it is assumed that the land will be planted with alfalfa, which has a nitrogen requirement of 150 to 200 lb/acre/year, or similar crops such as sorghum or corn, with slightly lower nitrogen requirements ranging from 100 to 150 lb/acre/year. Given that the nitrogen loading from the Biolac® system effluent is 117.2 lb/acre/year, this is below the typical nitrogen uptake rate for these crops. Therefore, the applied effluent is expected to be sufficiently absorbed by the crops, minimizing excess nitrogen percolation to groundwater and ensuring environmental compliance.

Since water balance studies indicate that there will be a period when the storage tank will be empty, alfalfa is the best choice due to its higher nitrogen removal efficiency and greater drought tolerance compared to the other crops. Alfalfa's deep root system allows it to withstand drier conditions, making it more resilient during potential dry spells when irrigation water might be limited.

For the irrigation of non-food crops such as alfalfa, Washington state regulations, as outlined in WAC 173-219-390, require the use of Class B reclaimed water. Class B reclaimed water is suitable

---

for non-food crops like alfalfa. A 50-foot setback from public access is required to minimize health risks.

Based on WAC 173-219-320, the minimum treatment standards required to qualify for Class B reclaimed water are outlined in Table 1 and Table 2 under WAC 173-219-330. These tables specify the performance standards that reclaimed water must meet after undergoing the necessary treatment processes, including biological oxidation and disinfection.

As shown in Table 1, the effluent quality requirements for land application are less stringent compared to those for discharge into surface water. As mentioned earlier, none of the parameters in Table 1: Minimum Biological Oxidation Performance Standards are limiting design parameters. The proposed limit for these parameters, as outlined in Table 3 Chapter 3 to discharge to Spangle Creek, requires a higher treatment level compared to secondary treatment and is more restrictive.

The only limiting design parameter is the total coliform count, as specified in Table 2: Class A and B Performance Standards, which states that:

The 7-day median shall not exceed 23 MPN/ml or CFU/ml

The sample maximum shall not exceed 240 MPN/ml or CFU/ml

While phosphorus is a key nutrient in wastewater, it is not a limiting design factor in land application through irrigation. Its removal is primarily governed by soil adsorption capacity, plant uptake, and microbial activity rather than specific treatment requirements. In contrast to nitrogen, which has strict regulatory limits due to groundwater contamination risks, phosphorus mobility in soil is relatively low, reducing its immediate impact on water quality. However, long-term phosphorus accumulation must still be considered, as excessive loading can lead to soil saturation, increasing the potential for surface runoff or groundwater transport. To mitigate this risk, effective management practices such as site selection, crop rotation, and periodic monitoring help ensure sustainable phosphorus retention while maintaining compliance with land application standards.

A side-by-side comparison of the proposed and permitted effluent limits for discharge into Spangle Creek versus the required treatment level for land application on non-food crops is presented in the table below. As shown, the treatment requirements for land application on non-food crops are significantly less stringent compared to the discharge limits for surface water. Given these differences, a detailed cost analysis of both alternatives is necessary to determine the most cost-effective and environmentally compliant method for effluent disposal.
