# 7.5 Biosolids Management and Land Application

Biosolids are the nutrient-rich organic materials resulting from the treatment of domestic sewage in a wastewater treatment facility. When properly treated and processed, biosolids can be safely recycled and applied as fertilizer to improve and maintain productive soils and stimulate plant growth.

## 7.5.1 Types of Biosolids and Regulatory Compliance

The Washington State Department of Ecology and the U.S. Environmental Protection Agency (EPA) classify biosolids based on their pathogen and metal content. Class B biosolids are treated to significantly reduce pathogens, while Class A biosolids are treated further to eliminate pathogens and meet more stringent standards for land application without site restrictions.

The proposed biosolids management plan for the Spangle treatment facility will comply with both state (WAC 173-308) and federal (40 CFR Part 503) regulations. Based on effluent quality and facility goals, it is assumed that biosolids will be stabilized and treated to Class B standards for agricultural land application.

## 7.5.2 Land Application of Biosolids

The land application of biosolids offers several benefits, including:

* Improved soil fertility due to the addition of organic matter and nutrients (nitrogen, phosphorus).
* Reduction in chemical fertilizer use.
* Enhanced water retention in soil.
* Recycling of waste materials in an environmentally sustainable way.

Sites for land application must be evaluated for suitability in terms of slope, soil permeability, proximity to groundwater, and crop type. The same Naff Silt Loam (6061) soil, already identified as suitable for wastewater effluent land application, is also considered ideal for biosolids application due to its infiltration capacity and low runoff potential.

## 7.5.3 Estimated Annual Biosolids Production

Assumption: For a domestic wastewater treatment plant, typical biosolids yields are estimated to be in the range of about 0.002 to 0.003 dry tons per 1,000 gallons of treated wastewater. In our calculation we used a midpoint value of 0.0025 dry tons per 1,000 gallons.

Reference:

Washington State Department of Ecology Biosolids Management Guidelines, Publication #93-80, Revised July 2000 (which describes typical biosolids production estimates and regulatory benchmarks).

EPA standards and guidance (40 CFR Part 503) also provide typical yield ranges for municipal wastewater treatment.

Using the conservative middle range:

0.0025 dry tons per 1,000 gallons

---

With 10,000 gpd, that's:

$$
12,054 \text{ gpd} \times \frac{0.0025 \, \text{dry tons}}{1,000 \, \text{gallons}} = 0.03 \text{ dry tons/day}
$$

Annual dry tons:

$$
0.03 \times 365 = 11 \text{ dry tons/year}
$$

### 7.5.4 Design Application Rate Based on Agronomic Needs

Assumption: Many programs use an agronomic application rate of about 4 to 5 dry tons per acre per year for biosolids when applied for agronomic benefits such as soil fertility improvements. A conservative rate of 4 dry tons/acre/year is assumed to protect against nutrient overload and meet regulatory guidance.

Reference:

Washington State Department of Ecology Biosolids Management Guidelines also provide recommended agronomic rates for biosolids applications depending on the crop and soil type.

Common practice guidelines (as referenced in technical documents like Sullivan, D. 1998. "Fertilizing with Biosolids") support these loading rates for land application.

$$
\text{Land required} = \frac{11 \, \text{dry tons/year}}{4 \, \text{dry tons/acre/year}} \approx 2.75 \text{ acres/year}
$$

### 7.5.5 Crop Recommendations for Biosolids Application

Assumption: Crops best suited for biosolids applications are those with high nitrogen demand and that are not intended for direct human consumption. Typically recommended options include:

* Grass hay or pasture grass: Common and proven for nutrient uptake while providing safe forage.
* Forage crops (e.g., wheat for silage, corn silage): Often used to maximize the nutrient use while remaining within safe regulatory limits.
* Reference:

Washington State Department of Ecology Biosolids Management Guidelines outline crop recommendations and management practices for biosolids use.

Additional agronomic literature and Cooperative Extension recommendations in Washington also advise on suitable crops for land-applied biosolids.

A covered storage facility would be needed for storing dewatered biosolids during the non-growing season, typically for about 6 months. With an annual generation of 11 dry tons of biosolids, the storage facility would need to accommodate the volume required for half the year,

---

which is 5.5 dry tons. Using a covered storage system, such as a roofed structure or a covered concrete pad, offers several advantages. It protects the biosolids from the elements, such as rain and snow, minimizing the risk of water infiltration, which could affect the biosolids' composition. Additionally, it reduces the potential for odor issues and helps maintain the biosolids in a more solid state compared to liquid storage.

Storage Calculation:

Dry tons per Year: 11 dry tons

Storage for 6 months: 11 / 2 = 5.5 dry tons to be stored.

Assuming the same density as the previous example (0.8 tons per cubic yard), the storage volume required would be:

$$
\text{Storage Volume} = \frac{5.5 \, dry \, tons}{0.8 \, per \, cubic \, yard} = 6.875 \text{ cubic yards}
$$

Therefore, the City of Spangle would need a covered storage facility with a capacity of at least 7 cubic yards to store the 5.5 dry tons of dewatered biosolids for 6 months. This covered facility would offer a reliable and efficient means of storing biosolids, ensuring proper handling and minimizing environmental impact.

### 7.6 Preliminary Planning Cost

In this section, the costs associated with land treatment application using the irrigation method are estimated. The developed estimates are generally expected to be within 30 percent of the actual cost, assuming favorable conditions for land application. However, if conditions are determined to be unfavorable, costs may increase by more than 50 percent. Such unfavorable conditions could include extensive site preparation, such as grading or the installation of a lift station to transfer water to the site, as well as stormwater management requirements.

The cost of land application has been divided into six major categories, as listed below:

1. Land  
2. Pre-application treatment  
3. Crop revenue  
4. Transmission  
5. Storage  
6. Application system  

In this cost estimation, we develop the initial capital cost estimate for the year 2025, which serves as the starting point of the design period. Additionally, we assume that capital costs for the near-term milestone (2031), mid-term milestone (2039), and final design year (2045) will be projected based on a hypothetical capital cost curve. This approach allows us to estimate future expenditures by accounting for anticipated cost escalations, inflation, and potential changes in project scope over time.

---

### 7.6.1 Land Costs

The cost of land is often a significant portion of the total project cost. Land may be acquired through an outright purchase, lease, or other means. In this estimate, it is assumed that the land will be purchased outright, and the cost is estimated based on the total land required at the prevailing market price. The market price of land is determined using data from the Spokane County Tax Assessor.

It is assumed that the land will include the required irrigation area, storage, buffer zones, access roads, maintenance roads, and buildings.

* Required Site Area: 4.7 acres  
* Required Storage Area: 1.72 acres  
* Total Net Site Area: 4.7 + 1.72 = 6.42 acres  
* Total Required Area (including roads, buffer zones, and buildings): 5.58 × 1.25 = 8 acres  

It is assumed that the total required land area will include a 25% increase to account for roads, buffer zones, and buildings, resulting in a total estimated area of 7 acres.

An existing parcel owned by the city, identified as parcel number 33333.9012, has an area of 11.84 acres, which exceeds the required land area. Therefore, no land acquisition will be necessary, and there will be no associated land purchase costs.

The land is appraised for $104,680 in 2025 for the 11.84-acre parcel, and the adjacent land to the north, parcel number 33333.9017, is a 54.01-acre vacant parcel appraised at $180,520. Based on these two land values, the average price per acre in the area is approximately $4,331.06. A recent transaction on Zillow indicates an average price of $7,700 per acre, which will be used as the basis for the cost estimate.

**Cost of Preapplication Treatment**  
The cost of wastewater treatment varies depending on the required discharge standards. The two treatment scenarios under consideration are discharge to Spangle Creek and land application for non-food crops. Each option has different effluent limits, affecting treatment complexity and associated costs.

1. Treatment Stringency and Cost Impact  
The primary factors influencing cost differences include:  
* Biochemical Oxygen Demand (BOD₅) & Total Suspended Solids (TSS): Spangle Creek requires stricter limits (8 mg/L BOD₅ and 22.5 mg/L TSS) compared to land application (30/45 mg/L), necessitating additional aeration and retention time.  
* Total Nitrogen & Total Phosphorus: The Spangle Creek discharge standard (1 mg/L N, 5.7 mg/L P) mandates advanced nutrient removal, which increases chemical and energy costs. Land application has no specific limits.  
* Fecal Coliform: Stricter disinfection requirements for Spangle Creek (100 organisms/100 mL) may necessitate higher UV dosage or chlorination, increasing operational costs.  
* Temperature & pH: Both discharge options require a pH range of 6.5–8.5, and temperature is not regulated for land application, meaning no significant cost difference for these parameters.


---

## 2. Estimated Cost Difference

The additional cost for Spangle Creek discharge arises from:

* Higher energy consumption for aeration, nutrient removal.
* Increased chemical usage for phosphorus removal.
* More stringent monitoring and regulatory compliance costs.

Using unit treatment costs from similar Biolac systems:

* Secondary Treatment (Biolac) Cost: ~$5.00 per 1,000 gallons.
* Additional Advanced Treatment for Spangle Creek Discharge: $2.50–$4.00 per 1,000 gallons.

For an average daily flow of 12,054 gallons per day (gdp):

Land Application Cost:  
$$(12,054 \div 1,000) \times 5.00 = 60.27 \text{ /day}$$

Spangle Creek Discharge Cost:  
$$(12,054 \div 1,000) \times (5.00 + 3.00) = 96.43 \text{ /day}$$  
(assuming an additional $3.00 per 1,000 gallons).

Annual Cost Difference:  
$$(96.43 - 60.27) \times 365 = 13,187 \text{ per year}$$

For discharge to Spangle Creek, additional phosphorus removal units are required, with an estimated capital cost of approximately $50,000 to $80,000 for a facility with an average daily flow of 12,054 GPD. The final cost will depend on site-specific conditions and the selected treatment technology, whereas no capital cost is required for phosphorus removal in land application.

Discharging to Spangle Creek is estimated to incur approximately $13,187 more in annual operational costs compared to land application due to stricter effluent limits, which necessitate additional treatment processes. A detailed cost breakdown can be further refined based on site-specific energy, chemical, and labor costs.

### Crop Revenues

The estimated net revenue for growing alfalfa on 4.7 acres in Washington State ranges between $7,750 and $9,600 per year. With an average yield of 8 tons per acre and a market price of $300 per ton, the total annual income is approximately $11,300. Production costs, including fertilization, pest control, harvesting, and labor, are estimated between $1,385 and $2,889 per year. In comparison, corn on 3.86 acres can result in a net revenue ranging from a profit of $1,009.25 to a loss of $920.75 per year, depending on production costs and yield. Given these figures, alfalfa offers a significantly higher and more consistent net revenue, making it the better choice for growers looking for a stable and profitable.

### Cost of Land Treatment Application

Different irrigation systems were evaluated for a 4 to 10-acre alfalfa or other no-food farm irrigation, including Side Roll Sprinklers, Center Pivot, Wheel Line Sprinklers, and Drip Irrigation. Side Roll Sprinklers were found to be the most cost-effective and flexible option, with lower installation and operational costs compared to center pivots and wheel line systems. Their mobility allows for efficient coverage of irregularly shaped fields, making them ideal for smaller acreages.

---

While Drip Irrigation offers high water efficiency, it may not be as cost-effective for larger fields due to high upfront material and installation costs. Additionally, Drip Irrigation is less effective for wastewater treatment using evapotranspiration, as it primarily delivers water to the root zone, missing out on the evapotranspiration benefits of wetting the plant leaves. This makes it less suitable for utilizing the natural evaporation processes that are beneficial for managing wastewater in certain agricultural systems.

Estimated Costs for Land Application with Side-Roll Sprinklers (4 to 10 Acres):

Installation Costs:

The cost of installing a side-roll sprinkler system for a 4 to 10-acre field ranges between $10,000 to $15,000. This includes the system setup, sprinklers, and the necessary infrastructure to get the system up and running.

Operational Costs:

Water Usage and Energy: The operational cost of water and energy will depend on the specific conditions of the field, but side-roll systems are known for their relatively low energy consumption, making the overall operational costs more manageable.

Labor Costs: While side-roll systems require more manual labor to move between different areas of the field compared to automated systems, labor costs for a 4 to 10-acre field would generally range between $500 and $2,000 per year, depending on the frequency of movement and field conditions. However, the labor cost has already been included in the crop production revenue and, therefore, will not be considered as a separate cost item for irrigation.

It was assumed that adding capacity to the storage lagoon will cost approximately one-third of the construction cost of a 75,000 SF bottom footprint lagoon.  

The required storage capacity for the year 2045 will need to be upgraded to accommodate increased demand. A 20% increase in capacity is required, with a pond bottom area of 90,000 SF.

# Appendix A: Equivalent Diameter Calculation for 0.7d Flow Conditions

**Introduction**

According to the Orange Book (The Criteria for Sewage Works Design by the Washington State Department of Ecology), the recommended design capacity for gravity sewer pipes is typically 0.7 times the pipe diameter (0.7d) under peak flow conditions. However, Autodesk Storm and Sanitary Analysis (SSA) calculates pipe capacity based on a full-flow condition (100% full diameter). To align with the 0.7d standard, an equivalent diameter was determined for use in the model.

**Calculation Methodology**

Given Data

* Flow depth in the original pipe: 0.7\(D\)
* Equivalent pipe flows full.
* Original pipe diameter: \(D = 8\) inches.

**Formulae Used**

Flow Rate Equation:

$$
Q = A V
$$

Where:

* \(Q\) = Flow rate
* \(A\) = Cross-sectional area
* \(V\) = Velocity

Hydraulic Radius Equations:

For a full-flowing pipe:

$$
R_{h2} = \frac{D}{4} = \frac{r_2}{2}
$$

For a partially full pipe:

$$
R_{h1} = \frac{A_1}{P_1}
$$

Manning’s Equation for Flow Velocity:

$$
V = \frac{1}{n} R_h^{2/3} S^{1/2}
$$

---

Where:

$$n = \text{Manning’s roughness coefficient}$$

$$S = \text{Slope}$$

Area and Wetted Perimeter for the Partially Full Pipe

The central angle \(\theta\) for 70% depth:

$$\theta = 2 \arccos(-0.4) \approx 3.89 \text{ radians}$$

Flow area:

$$A_1 = \frac{r^2}{2} \left( \theta - \sin \theta \right) = \frac{4^2}{2} (3.89 - \sin(3.89))$$

$$A_1 \approx 35.71$$

Wetted perimeter:

$$P_1 = r \theta = 4 \times 3.89 \approx 15.56$$

Hydraulic Radius for the Partially Full Pipe

$$R_{h1} = \frac{A_1}{P_1} = \frac{35.71}{15.56} \approx 2.3 \text{ in}$$

Hydraulic Radius for the Full Pipe

$$R_{h2} = \frac{r_2}{2}$$

Equate Flow Rates Using Manning’s Equation

$$A_1 R_{h1}^{2/3} = A_2 R_{h2}^{2/3}$$

$$35.71 (2.3)^{2/3} = \pi r_2^2 \left(\frac{r_2}{2}\right)^{2/3}$$

Equivalent Radius

$$r_2 \approx 0.456 \times 8$$

$$r_2 \approx 3.74 \text{ inches}$$

Conclusion

The equivalent radius of a full-flowing pipe that matches the capacity of an 8-inch pipe flowing at 70% depth is 3.74 inches, which corresponds to an equivalent diameter of 7.48 inches.

*The equivalent radius of the pipe is approximately \(0.456D\)*

---

# Appendix B: Appendix: Detailed Calculations for Projected Population

## 1. Organic Growth (Without Wheatland Estates Development)

**Formula Used:**  
Projected Population = Total Housing Units × Occupancy Rate × Average Household Size

**For 2031:**  
* Forecasted Housing Units (Scenario 1 - Organic Growth): 135 units  
* Occupancy Rate: 96.4% (0.964)  
* Average Household Size: 2.28 people  

Projected Population (2031) = 135 × 0.964 × 2.28 = 297 people

**For 2031:**  
* Forecasted Housing Units (Scenario 1 - Organic Growth): 137 units  
* Occupancy Rate: 96.4% (0.964)  
* Average Household Size: 2.28 people  

Projected Population (2031) = 137 × 0.964 × 2.28 = 301 people

**For 2045:**  
* Forecasted Housing Units (Scenario 1 - Organic Growth): 150 units  
* Occupancy Rate: 96.4% (0.964)  
* Average Household Size: 2.28 people  

Projected Population (2045) = 150 × 0.964 × 2.28 = 330 people

## 2. Scenario Two: Wheatland Estates Development

---

**Formula Used:**  
Projected Population = Total Housing Units × Occupancy Rate × Average Household Size  
Given:  
Occupancy Rate = 96.4% (0.964)  
Average Household Size = 2.28 people

For 2039: Projected Population (2039) = \(257 \times 0.964 \times 2.28 = 565.9 \approx 566\) people  
For 2045: Projected Population (2045) = \(320 \times 0.964 \times 2.28 = 735.7 \approx 736\) people  

# Appendix C: GIS System for the Town of Spangle Sewer Plan

**Overview**  
The Town of Spangle’s General Sewer Plan incorporates a GIS system designed to enhance sewer planning and management. This user-friendly system connects sewer modeling software, such as WaterGEMS and InfoWorks, with advanced mapping tools, providing accurate infrastructure mapping, facilitating analysis and assessment, and improving future development planning.

The GIS system also has the potential to integrate with essential tools, including hydraulic and hydrological modeling, customer service and billing, maintenance management, laboratory information, SCADA systems, document workflow, asset management, field data collection, inspections, and Capital Improvement Programs (CIP) for planning and construction monitoring. This comprehensive integration optimizes resource allocation, streamlines management, and supports data-driven decision-making for the town’s sewer infrastructure.

Goals of the GIS System

1. Simplifying Sewer Model Setup and Maintenance

---

* The system makes it faster and easier to build and update sewer models.

2. Improving Decision-Making  
* With clear maps and data analysis, the town can make better choices about sewer projects.

3. Planning for the Future  
* The system uses accurate data to help plan for growth and new development.

The GIS system is an important step forward for the Town of Spangle. It helps manage the current sewer system more efficiently while preparing for future needs.

Data Collection and Integration Efforts  
To support the General Sewer Plan, significant efforts were made to gather and integrate accurate data, providing a reliable foundation for effective sewer planning and management.

Land Base Development and Data Accuracy  
The GIS system utilizes the ArcGIS Pro built-in basemap, configured with the Washington State Plane coordinate system (NAD83 Washington North datum) to ensure spatial accuracy.

Manhole locations and rim data were primarily collected using GPS technology, with the exception of specific manholes—MH1 (lift station), MH8, MH45, and MH1A. Additionally, the wastewater treatment facility was surveyed using total station methods, ensuring precise measurements.  
To further enhance accuracy, GPS survey data was tied to two WSDOT monuments (IDs: 4370 & 4371) within the US-195 right-of-way. Historical as-built drawings from 1976, 1997, and 2018 (sourced from the PARIS website) supplemented the collected data, providing essential information on pipe dimensions, slopes, and invert elevations. Manholes were standardized to a 48-inch diameter, with cleanouts assumed to be 6 inches. The 4-inch force main, for which complete records were unavailable, was modeled based on partial drawings and specific elevation assumptions.

Data Processing and Challenges  
Topographical details were enhanced using Lidar data sourced from the Washington State Department of Natural Resources Lidar portal. Spokane County parcel maps defined city limits, while the Urban Growth Boundary was delineated using shapefiles from the Spokane County GIS data catalog.

Challenges arose with incomplete records for certain manholes (MH8, MH45, and MH1A), whose locations were approximated using aerial imagery and record drawings. Similarly, limited force main data required assumptions to fill gaps in the dataset.

GIS Integration  
All collected data was meticulously integrated into the ArcGIS system. This included creating and linking data tables for pipes and manholes to their respective spatial features. Additional functionality was added by linking related documents, such as the lift station record drawings, directly to the corresponding GIS features in PDF format.

The integrated GIS platform now serves as a centralized resource for managing sewer infrastructure data, streamlining planning and decision-making processes.
